We supposed the mountain must have been quite deserted from the myriads that had flown away; but lifting the glass to it, as we were leaving its border, we were appalled to find it still alive with the myriads that were left behind. They kept leaving and leaving until our steamer got far beyond the Craig, and till we could no longer discern their departure with the telescope.
</p><p>"And it was miles off into the dusky Irish Sea, before we saw the ebbing of their mighty movement, and that they were beginning to return. We felt relieved to see them going back. It had scarcely occurred to us in our surprise, that they were not</p></blockquote>
<p></p><center>-40-</center><hr>

<blockquote>leaving their native cliffs forever. Slowly and sadly they seemed to return, while the eye sought in vain to ken the outskirts of their mighty caravan. And Ailsa Craig had sunk far into our rear, and quite sensibly diminished in the distance, before the rearmost of the feathered host had disappeared from our sight.
<p>"The excitement occasioned us considerable depression of spirits, from which we were not entirely relieved until night came down upon the St. George's Channel, and the protracted northern twilight could no longer disclose objects to our wearied vision. Then after refreshing ourselves with some substantial confectionery, with which dear George Thompson had kindly stuffed our pockets from a shop at Greenock, before leaving "the land of cakes," our beloved fellow-passenger and ourself, after sundry fond remembrances of the other side of the ocean, some expectations of next day's greeting in Dublin, and some grateful sense, as we trust, of the goodness that had not forgotten us amid all our dangers by sea and land, we forgot what we had seen, and whereabouts we were, in the arms of oblivious sleep."</p></blockquote>


<p><img src="nprogers.jpg" align="left" width="393" height="621">
To do justice to the memory of Nathaniel Peabody Rogers, to his character and work, would require genius and inspiration like his own.
</p><p>Nor, perhaps, would this cheap age even then understand nor comprehend it.
</p><ul>It manufactures sham and shoddy at too many of its mills, political, literary, social, moral and religious.
<p>It quotes Pope and Burns about an "honest man," but seems not to know him when he comes.
</p><p>It celebrated the birthday of [Scottish poet] Robert Burns [1759-1796] with much pomp and demonstration in less than one month after it hung [abolitionist] <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#rescue">John Brown</a> [1800-1859] for a heroism and devotion to freedom and humanity, which began, rekindled with divine fervor, where the zeal of LaFayette for a white man's liberty paled out of human sight.</p></ul>
<p>
<br>
<br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br></p><p>
</p><ul>And socially, morally and religiously it [this "cheap age"] had hung Rogers long before, in the same</ul>
<p></p><center>-41-</center><hr>

persecuting spirit that burned his illustrious ancestor [the first English Protestant martyr, Rev. John Rogers (1500-1555] in the [1555] Smithfield pyre [under English Queen Mary (1553-1558)].
<p>In the true spirit of martyrdom did Rogers, like John Brown, join the anti-slavery movement in an hour of peril. Garrison had been mobbed in Boston, as was said, "in broad day, by Boston's best men in broadcloth, gentlemen of property and standing"; driven from a female anti-slavery concert of prayer which he had been invited to attend and address. Mr. Garrison said of the spectacle when all the streets near the place of meeting were thronged with a mob burning with murderous intent:
</p><blockquote>"It was an awful, sublime and soul-thrilling scene—enough, one would suppose, to melt adamantine hearts, and make even fiends of darkness stagger and retreat.   Indeed the clear, untremulous voice of that Christian heroine, Miss Parker, in prayer occasionally awed the ruffians into silence, and she was heard distinctly, even in the midst of their hisses and yells and curses."</blockquote>Garrison withdrew from the prayer meeting and the mayor entered in obedience to the wishes of the fiendish crew, and dispersed it.  Then the cry, the shriek, the yell was,<blockquote><dd>"we must have Garrison." "Out with him! Lynch him!"</dd></blockquote>Some of the rioters discovered and seized him.   They drew him furiously to a window and were about to thrust him out, when one of them relented and said,<blockquote><dd>"Let us not kill him out-right."</dd></blockquote>But they coiled a rope about his body, nearly stripped him of his clothing, then dragged him through the streets till he was finally rescued by <i>posse comitatus</i> and at frightful peril was at length got to the mayor's office.   There he was provided with clothing and from thence sent to jail, as "a disturber of the peace," the mayor and his advisers declaring that "the only way to preserve his life."
<p>In Alton
</p><p></p><center>-42-</center><hr>

<a name="p43"></a>Rev. Elijah Parish Lovejoy, too, another anti-slavery editor, had been shot and killed by a mob, five bullets being taken from his body, three from his breast, and that, too, in 1837, only a few months before Mr. Rogers removed with his family to Concord to conduct the <i>Herald of Freedom</i>.
<p></p><center><table width="340" bordercolor="tan" border="4" bgcolor="ccffff"><tbody><tr><td align="center"><b>Ed. Note: See also <a href="#p84">p 84</a>. Harriet Beecher Stowe, <i>Key</i> (1853), <a href="keytom.htm#p228-censorship-murder">p 228</a>, provides more details on Rev. Lovejoy's murder, in reprisal for exercising First Amendment freedom-of-the-press rights.</b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>

<p>So that in assuming such position, he also, as might be said, "took his life in his hand." For Concord itself was no stranger to the mob at that time and for years afterward was the consecrated guardian of slavery.
</p><p>As a member of the Plymouth Congregational church, both Mr. and Mrs. Rogers had cooperated earnestly, faithfully in works of religious benevolence and charity. But when they demanded that those in bonds in their own country should be remembered even "<a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?Hebrews+13:3">as bound with them</a>," they were repulsed as disorderly, contumacious disturbers of the peace of the church and its minister, who, at that time, was among the most virulent opposers of the whole anti-slavery enterprise.
</p><p>But they did not withdraw from their church connection till they saw that southern slaveholders were more welcome to the pulpit and sacramental table, than were faithful, devoted abolitionists, whose moral and religious integrity of character, as well as soundness of opinion, were above reproach or suspicion.  Rogers, beyond most public men, ever had unshaken faith in the people, though conservative while a politician, and orthodox in his religious faith. When he left the church he investigated its character anew and for himself. The claims of the clergy to prerogative in things temporal as well as spiritual, he soon learned to hold in profound disesteem. To no one man then living, or who has appeared since, does the world owe more than to him for exposing and rebuking the
</p><p></p><center>-43-</center><hr>

arrogance and insolence not to say down-right fraud and dishonesty, of a ministry whose ruling, directing power in all the great popular demonstrations of the land, north as well as south, was exerted in support and sanctification of slavery. The exceptions to this charge were too few to change the result, as will appear in the progress of this work.
<p>Mr. Rogers never doubted for a moment that the people, well and wisely taught, would abolish slavery and cease to oppress one another. And so like the Great Emancipator of Nazareth, he directed all his sternest strokes and rebukes at the priests and rulers, who really "bound the heavy burdens and laid them on men's shoulders [<a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?Matthew+23:4">Matthew 23:4</a>]," as in Judea, two thousand years ago. He and his associates of the Garrison school of abolitionists relied solely on the power of moral and spiritual truth to rescue the slave as well as to redeem and save the world.
</p><p>They formed, they joined no political party.  They abjured the ballot altogether as a reforming or restoring agency, as much as they did the bullet, the only specie redemption of the ballot, in every government of force. Both Mr. and Mrs. Rogers were members and officers of the New England Non-Resistance Society. And none ever more highly adorned the doctrine of their profession than they.
</p><p>As one with vision anointed to perceive all moral and spiritual truth, Rogers seemed to stand almost alone. His editorial writings are witness to this, and will be to more than the next generation. It were well for man and womankind, if whole volumes of them, judiciously selected, could be reproduced and scattered everywhere, like the shining constellation among the dimmer stars.  His words to-day are, many of them, wondrously fresh and new.
</p><p></p><center>-44-</center><hr>

<p>The temperance cause had no firmer or mure consistent friend. The peace societies had best of reasons to be proud of his support, in word and deed. To him human life was sacred as the life of God. Once, at a grand Peace gathering, it was strenuously argued by most of the members who spoke, that human life could and should be taken by divine command.   And the president of the society himself made an argument in defence of all the slaughters of the Canaanites and other tribes and peoples, men, women and children, by <a href="http://www.aish.com/literacy/jewishhistory/Crash_Course_in_Jewish_History_Part_9_Moses.asp">Moses</a>, Joshua and their destroying hosts, because perpetrated by command of God.
</p><p>It was at one of the last meetings Rogers ever attended, and he was then too feeble to bear an active part m the deliberations. But after listening a good while to scripture text and learned logic under Levitical law, he rose to his feet and in low voice asked:  "Does our brother yonder say that if God commanded him, he would take a sword and use it in slaying human beings, and innocent, helpless human beings? "Yes, if God commanded," was the answer. "Well, I wouldn't," responded Rogers, and sank back into his seat, amid loud cheers of evident approval and admiration.
</p><p>Woman, to him, was in all rights, privileges and prerogatives, the full equal of man.   He was a Christian in the divinest, sublimest sense of that still mysterious and much abused word. And as such his kingdom was not of this world. And so he could neither vote in, nor ask others to vote in nor to fight for any government based on military power.
</p><p>As husband and father, none ever knew one in whom his family were more supremely felicitated. As companion and friend, blessed and happy were all those who enjoyed his confidence and esteem. Gentle,
</p><p></p><center>-45-</center><hr>

simple, tender, kind, ready to sacrifice his own comfort; sharing on occasion, like General Washington, his room and bed with a colored man, and yet always discriminating in high degree; with tastes most refined; ever ready to criticise, even censure a friend, however dear, when he deemed it just and
demanded; firm as his own Ailsa Craig, whenever or wherever, or however a moral principal was in jeopardy; running over with music, poetry, and culture of every kind, he was a man, the like of whom the world has seldom seen—may not soon see again.
<p></p><center>-46-<hr>
<a name="p47"></a>CHAPTER III.
<p>SLAVERY—AS IT WAS.</p></center>

<p>Everybody now is anti-slavery. It is honorable now to be a child of the man who "cast the first anti-slavery vote in our town;" or called "our first anti-slavery meeting;" or first entertained Garrison as guest, or <a href="bulwarks.htm#akct">Abby Kelley</a>, or <a href="douglassuos.htm">Frederick Douglass</a>; or rescued Stephen Foster or Lucy Stone from the hands of a ferocious mob; or raised, or commanded the first company of colored troops in the war of Rebellion, at the time when not a musical band could be found in the whole city of New York to play for a colored regiment, as it marched from the New Haven Railway station to the steamer at the foot of Canal street to embark for the seat of war!

</p><p><a name="p47-vile-tyng"></a>"<i>Paid pipers</i>" the <a href="patton1846.htm#listvileclergy">venerable Dr. Tyng</a> with withering scorn called them all on the same evening in Cooper Institute, where he presided at a lecture by George William Curtis. "Paid pipers," with wind too immaculate to blow away in escort of a gallant battalion of our country's saviors, "when there was no other name under heaven given among men," whereby the nationality could be saved but the <i>negro name</i>; despised as he was and rejected of men; "a man of sorrows" and acquainted all his dreary life with grief!

</p><p>Everybody now is [claims to be] an abolitionist, or son, or grandson of an anti-slavery parentage, and so all seem to claim equal honor, so far as honor is due, for ridding the world of the sublimest scourge and curse that ever afflicted the human race.
</p><p></p><center>-47-</center><hr>


<p>Few now, however, have much conception of what slavery was; or what was genuine, effective anti-slavery, when <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#tobaccofarmers">slavery sat supreme</a> "on its throne of skulls," and ruled the whole nation, state, church and school, literature, trade, commerce, manufactures and agriculture, as with rod of iron!

</p><p>And its first command, great command, only command was, "Thou shalt have no god but me." Not, as from Mount Sinai, "no other <i>gods</i> before me," but no other <i>god</i>. Not "no other gods <i>before</i> me," but "no other gods <i>with</i>, or <i>above</i> or <i>below</i> me!" So it was.  Anti-slavery then, was more than a name; more than profession; or denomination in religion; or party in the government.
</p><p>So Christianity had mighty meanings when the great apostle to the Gentiles wrote: "I am not ashamed of the gospel of Christ." [<a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?Romans+1:16">Rom 1:16</a>.] And "I determined to know nothing among you save Jesus Christ and him crucified." [<a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?1Corinthians+2:2">I Cor. 2:2</a>.] It had fearful meanings when the gardens of Nero were illumined with the burning bodies of martyred saints, both men and women, young and old! When to name the Christ of God was death in lingering torments—when crucifixions were so multiplied that, as in grim epigram it was said, "space was wanted for crosses, and crosses for christians." And yet so sublime was Christian heroism at that hour, that it could have well been added, but christians are never wanting for crosses.
</p><p>But what was our slave system, that so many now proudly claim to have aided to destroy? And whose fathers and mothers were those who really did bear active, effective part in the thirty years moral and peaceful conflict, inaugurated by Garrison with "<a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?Ephesians+6:17">sword of the spirit</a>;" whose only weapons were
</p><p></p><center>"The mild arms of truth and love,
<br>Made mighty through the living God?"</center>
<p></p><center>-48-</center><hr>


Or whose sons and brothers rushed at last to the field of mortal combat, and fought the bloodiest, mightiest, everyway, most frightful war, that has shaken the earth and darkened the skies in all the Christian years? <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#who">Slavery</a>! What is it? What was it on the American plantation? "Peculiar Institution," some called it.  "Patriarchal Institution," others!
<p>But what was it? All language pales and is silent in its dread presence. Slave-holding! "Deed without a name!" In cant phrase we said slavery degrades man to the brute, sinks woman to the dead level of the horse. And then who knows the height and depth, the length and breadth of those stunning words; insulting blasphemies against the Holy Spirit of Humanity!
</p><p>Let one advertisement, distributed by large handbills, as well as published in the daily newspapers of New Orleans, aid the imagination :
</p><p></p><center><table border="3" width="65%"><tbody><tr><td bgcolor="ddeeee"><b><font size="4">R</font>AFFLE. M<font size="2">R. </font><b>J<font size="2">OSEPH </font><b>J<font size="2">ENNINGS</font><b> respectfully informs his friends and the public that, at the request of many acquaintances, he has been induced to purchase from Mr. Osborne, of Missouri, the celebrated DARK BAY HORSE, "STAR," aged five years, square trotter and warranted sound, with a new, light Trotting Buggy and Harness; Also the dark, stout Mulatto Giri, "Sarah," aged about twenty years, general house servant, valued at <i>nine hundred dollars</i>, and guaranteed: and will be RAFFLED for at four o'clock <font size="2">P.M.</font><b>, February first, at the selection hotel of the subscribers. The above is as represented, and those persons who may wish to engage in the usual practice of raffling will, I assure them, be perfectly satisfied with their destiny in this affair.
<p>The whole is valued at its just worth, fifteen hundred dollars; fifteen hundred CHANCES at One Dollar each. The Raffle will be conducted by gentlemen selected by the interested subscribers present. Five nights will be allowed to complete the Raffle. Both of the above described can be seen at my store, No. 78 Common street, second door from Camp, at from nine o'clock <font size="2">A.M.</font><b>, to two <font size="2">P.M.</font><b>
</b></b></p><p><b><b>Highest throw to take the first choice; the lowest throw the remaining prize, and the fortunate winners will pay Twenty Dollars each for the refreshments furnished on the occasion.
</b></b></p><p><b><b>N. B. No chances recognized unless paid for previous to the commencement.
</b></b></p><center><dd><b><b>JOSEPH JENN1NGS.
</b></b></dd></center></b></b></b></b></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>In the light of a spectacle like this, it is possible to fancy slightly what should be understood when it is said that slavery degrades human beings to the plane of brute beasts.
</p><p>Or reverse the order of illustration, if we dare, and imagine a brute beast raised to the dignity and honor,
</p><p></p><center>-49-</center><hr>

the privilege and prerogative of a man, an immortal being. History or fable tells us of a Roman Sovereign who made a favorite horse first Consul of the Empire. Such mockery might have been. But suppose in a Christian country, in a Christian sanctuary, it were proposed to admit, not a horse, but some dogs into full fellowship and communion with the church. It is on a delightful Sunday of early summer, in a pleasant New England country town. The village gardens are already abloom with early flowers, the orchards are white with prophecy of abundant fruit, and every tree is an orchestra of cheerful birds, whose worship-notes almost charm the Sabbath silence into sweet accord with the songs of paradise.
<p>All the village and the districts around assemble at their, to them, "house of God." At the appointed hour, the baptized communicants of the accepted faith are invited to seats at the sacramental board. The unregenerate of the congregation retire to the outer seats, paying silent but respectful attention. The first scene in the solemn service is admission of new members, who are invited forward to the altar. There, in presence of the congregation, they listen and bow silent assent to the Articles of Faith and the Covenant Vows, and receive the seal of baptism, in the name of the triune God.  Solemn and impressive as this may be, it may excite no unusual emotions, being neither new nor infrequent.
</p><p><a name="p50-degradation"></a>But slavery, we used to say with lip only, "degrades man and woman to a level with the brutes;" puts the "bay horse, Star," and the "Mulatto girl, Sarah," into the same raffle, or on the same auction-block. Now change the order. Elevate the brutes to the place of immortal beings at the baptismal font and sacramental table. Whistle up two or three dogs and solemnly read over to them the creed and
</p><p></p><center>-50-</center><hr>

covenant, and sprinkle them with the holy drops of baptism, calling them by their appropriate brute names, "Lion, I baptize thee in the name of the Father, and of the Son, and of the Holy Ghost. Tiger, I baptize thee in the name of the Father, and of the Son, and of the Holy Ghost." And let the third be a female: "Topsy, I baptize thee in the name of the Father, and of the Son, and of the Holy Ghost. Amen."
<p>Let such a spectacle be enacted on a delightful summer Sunday afternoon, in a beautiful New England village, in its pleasant white meeting-house, and at the memorial supper of that crucified Redeemer in whom the church and its pastor devoutly believed, and through whom they humbly hoped for salvation. Can the effect on the beholders of such a daring spectacle be described, or even imagined? As well, but no better, attempt a description of that slavery which truly did degrade human beings to a level with horses and with dogs.
</p><p>This whole scene was once supposed as illustration, in the days of slavery, in just such town and house of worship as here described, and not only that town, but the pulpit and religious press of both the hemispheres almost shrieked as with holy horror at what they called so audacious, so diabolical blasphemy.  And the cry came up from near and far for immediate punishment of him who had so illumined slavery, to the fullest demand of the statute, which was long confinement, it was held, in the State prison!
</p><p>But one thing was made clear. The words, Slavery degrades man to a level with beasts, were seen and felt as perhaps never before. The congregation where the illustration was presented saw and solemnly felt that from beasts up to men—to men exalted to angelic heights—was no farther than those deeps down which
</p><p></p><center>-51-</center><hr>

immortal man is plunged, to reach the level of the beasts that perish. And that frightful pit was reached by every chattel slave ever born.

<p><a name="p52-robbery"></a>But the question, What was American slavery? is not yet answered. To call it robbery, by only our dictionary definition, would pay it high compliment. Its fell [evil, <a href="#clergy=atheists">atheist</a>, <a href="abolitionists.htm#demonized-south">demonized</a>] work began where all ordinary robbery leaves off.
</p><p><img src="jwesley.gif" width="100" height="114" align="left" hspace="6">
John Wesley saw it [American slavery] and pronounced it, "<a href="goodellsaas.htm#p27-john-wesley">Sum of all villainies</a>."
</p><p>And if he did not pronounce <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#tobaccofarmers">the slave holder</a> sum of all villains, he did address him in words like these:
</p><p><br></p><center><table width="500" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>"What I have said to slave-traders, equally concerns <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#tobaccofarmers">all slave-holders</a>, of whatever rank and degree, seeing man-buyers are exactly on a level with man-stealers. You say, I pay honestly for my goods, and am not concerned to know they are honestly come by.
<p>"Nay, but you are.  *   *   *  You know they are not honestly come by; you know they are procured by means nothing near so innocent as picking pockets,  house-breaking, or robbery on the highway. You <a href="cigadsillegal.htm#ignorance">know</a> they are procured by a <a href="definitions.htm#natural">deliberate species</a> of more complicated villainy, of fraud, robbery and murder, than was ever practiced by Mohammedans or Pagans; in particular, by murders of all kinds; by the blood of the innocent poured upon the ground like water.
</p><p>"Now it is <i>your</i> money that pays the African butcher. <i>You</i>, therefore, are principally guilty of all these frauds, robberies and murders. <i>You</i> are the spring that puts all the rest in motion. They would not stir a step without <i>you</i>; therefore the blood of all these wretches who die before their time lies upon <i>your</i> head. "The blood of thy brother crieth against thee from the earth." [<a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?Genesis+4:10">Gen. 4:10</a>]
</p><p>"O, whatever it costs, put a stop to its cry before it be too late; instantly, at any price, were it the half of your goods, deliver thyself from blood-guiltiness! Thy hands, thy bed, thy furniture, thy house, and thy lands, at present are stained with blood. Surely it is enough; accumulate no more guilt; spill no more the blood of the innocent. Do not hire another to shed blood; do not pay him for</p></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p></p><center>-52-</center><hr>

<center><table width="480" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>doing it. Whether you are a Christian or not, show yourself a man! Be not more savage than a lion or a bear."</b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>

<p>Slavery is not robbery therefore, because it is so much more, and worse. Indeed, to rob man of manhood, and beastialize him down with not only animals, but the dead matter on which brutes feed and tread, makes any farther spoliation simply impossible.
</p><p><a name="p52-adultery"></a>Or shall we pronounce American slavery adultery, wholesale, unblushing adultery? If not, it must be because, as with robbery, it was something so much worse. For, first, what is adultery but setting aside all rights, privileges and responsibilities, human and divine, of both the marriage and parental relations?  Slavery knew no more of marriage and parentage among slaves than among swine. Logically, as well as legally, it could not.  And the statutes and court decisions so declared.
</p><p><a name="p53-query"></a><img src="birneyjg.jpg" align="left" width="191" height="243" alt="James G. Birney">But such abomination had not only state sanction, but church sanctification as well. Judge Birney, of Kentucky, once a slave-holder, in his memorable tract entitled: "<i><a href="bulwarks.htm#p26-query">The American Churches, the Bulwarks of American Slavery</a></i><a href="bulwarks.htm#p26-query">," second edition <font size="2">[pp 26-27]</font></a>, revised by the author, cites this instance:

</p><p></p><center><table width="390" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>"In 1835 the following query referring to slaves was presented to the [<a href="#clergy=atheists">atheist</a>, <a href="abolitionists.htm#demonized-south">demonized</a>] Savannah River Baptist Association of Ministers: "Whether in case of involuntary separation of such a character as to preclude all prospect of future intercourse, the parties ought to be allowed to marry again.'"</b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>


<p>The following was the answer:
</p><p></p><center><table width="520" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>"* * * Such separation among persons situated as are our slaves, is civilly a separation by death.  And we believe that in the sight of God, it would be so viewed! *    *    *    The slaves are not free agents, and a dissolution by death is not more entirely without their consent and beyond their control than by such separation."</b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>


<p></p><center><table bgcolor="ccffff" border="2" bordercolor="purple"><tbody><tr><td><b>Ed. Note: See also other analyses of this, by, e.g.,
<li>Deacon James Birney, <i>Bulwarks</i> (1840), <a href="bulwarks.htm#p26-query">pp 26-27</a>
</li><li>Rev. William Goodell, <i>Slavery and Anti-Slavery</i> (1852), <a href="goodellsaas.htm#p185q">p 185</a>
</li><li>Harriet Beecher Stowe, <i>Key</i> (1853), <a href="keytom.htm#p205-sale-death">p 205</a>
</li><li>Rep. Owen Lovejoy, "The Barbarism of Slavery" (5 April 1860), <a href="lovejoybarbarism.htm#p204b">p 204b</a>
</li><li>Sen. Charles Sumner, "The Barbarism of Slavery" (4 June 1860) <a href="sumnerbarbarism.htm#p132-char2">p 312</a>.
</li></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p></p><center>-53-</center><hr>

<p>James G. Birney was at one time a slaveholder as well as judge in the courts, and a ruling elder in the Presbyterian church. He was induced to emancipate his slaves, as well as to provide for their future support, taking them over into the free state of Ohio for that purpose, by the faithful and earnest argument and appeal of <a href="weldslaveryasis.htm">Theodore D. Weld</a>, an early, eloquent and everyway most efficient apostle and laborer in the anti-slavery field.
</p><p>Washing his own hands from the blood and guilt of slave holding, Judge Birney set himself to the work of abolishing the foul system. Among his first endeavors was an attempt to purify the churches, beginning with his own. But neither his official standing in both state and church, nor his high consequent social status availed to shield him from every possible indignity and outrage at the hands of infuriated mobs, composed largely sometimes of members of the churches. Driven from Kentucky he removed to Ohio.
</p><p>His descent on Cincinnati, where he had now become known, was a signal to waken all the vengeance of both church and state against him. Meetings were at once called, "to see if the people will permit abolition papers to be published in this city." At the first meeting the postmaster, who was also a minister, presided. A committee of thirteen, all eminent citizens, and eight of them church members, was appointed to wait on Mr. Birney and assure him that his paper must stop, or the meeting would not be responsible for the consequences of its continuance. The chairman of the committee declared that "if the paper were not promptly suspended, a mob, unusual in numbers, determined in purpose, and desolating in its ravages, would be inevitable!" All of which proved true, for the paper did not stop. In the darkness of midnight the mob entered and carried
</p><p></p><center>-54-</center><hr>

press, types and all else of contents and sunk them in the Ohio river. And twice afterwards was the same outrage perpetrated. No wonder Mr. Burney entitled his memorable tract, published at the time, "<i><a href="bulwarks.htm">The American Churches the Bulwarks of American Slavery</a></i>." For the title was more than justified on every subsequent page, as will hereafter be made to appear.
<p>And the word of divine truth uttered by Mr. Weld, and the baptism of fire and water three times administered by the fiendish mob, with full approval of state, church and pulpit, were sufficient consecration of the author of the memorable tract to his subsequent anti-slavery ministry and apostleship.
</p><p>But returning to the argument. Not only was slavery adultery, as sanctified and committed by the churches, in thus sundering all marriage rights and responsibilities; it was legally and in solemn compact annihilation of human marriage and parentage. The court decisions contained sentiments such as these: "With consent of their masters, slaves may marry; but in a state of slavery it can produce no <i>civil</i> effect, because slaves are deprived of all civil rights." [<i>Judge Matthews of Louisana</i>].  Attorney-General Delany, of Maryland, held that slaves would not be admonished for incontinence, or punished for adultery or fornication; or prosecuted for petty treason, or for killing a husband, being a slave. The code of Louisiana declared,</p><blockquote>"a slave could not contract matrimony.  The association which takes place among slaves, and is called marriage, being properly designated <i>contubernium</i>, a relation without sanctity, and to which no civil rights adhere."</blockquote>So the plain, unquestionable fact was, slavery was wholesale, legalized, sanctified concubinage, or adultery, from first to last. Our government was based on the prostrate bodies, souls, and civil,
<p></p><center>-55-</center><hr>

social, marital, parental, educational, moral and religious rights of half a million of immortal beings. In three-quarters of a century their numbers multiplied till at the downfall of the institution there were four millions, and not one legal marriage ever existed in all their generations! And yet, compelled by law thus to live and herd like brute beasts, hundreds of thousands of them were admitted to baptism and sacramental communion and fellowship in all the great evangelical denominations in the land!
<p>One other attribute of <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#tobaccofarmers">the dreadful system</a> remains to be exposed, and that was <i><a href="definitions.htm#murder">murder</a></i>. Under the written law of slavery, more than seventy offences, when committed by slaves, were punishable with death. One law read, "if any slave shall presume to strike any white person, such slave may be lawfully killed." Of course killed on the spot. A woman or girl would have been killed (undoubtedly many were killed) for defending her person against the lustful attack of her overseer or other white assailant.
</p><p>Special laws existed for recapturing <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#rescue">escaped slaves</a> at any cost of life to the victims, by first proclaiming them outlaws. The following legal instrument with its accompaniments will suffice to show the way:

</p><p></p><center><table border="3" width="65%"><tbody><tr><td bgcolor="ddeeee"><b>
<table><tbody><tr><td width="47%"></td><td><b>STATE OF NORTH CAROLINA,</b></td><td><b>}
</b></td></tr><tr><td></td><td><b>Lenoir County,</b></td><td><b>}</b></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Whereas, complaint hath been this day made to us, two of the Justices of the Peace for the said county, by William D. Cobb, of Jones county, that two negro slaves belonging to him, named Ben (commonly known by the name of Ben Fox) and Rigdon, have absented themselves from their said master's service's and are lurking about in the counties of Lenoir and Jones, committing acts of felony;—these are, in the name of the State, to command the said slaves forthwith to surrender themselves, and return home to their said master.
</p><p>And we do hereby, by virtue of an act of the Assembly of this State, concerning servants and slaves, intimate and declare, if the said slaves do not surrender themselves and return home to their master immediately after the publication of these presents, <i>that any person may kill and destroy said slaves by such means as he or they think fit, without accusation or impeachment of any crime or offence for so doing, or without incurring any penalty or forfeiture thereby</i>.
</p><p>Given under our hands and seals, this 12th day of November, 1836.
<table><tbody><tr><td width="56%"></td><td><b><font size="2">
B. COLEMAN, J. P. [Seal.]
</font></b></td></tr><tr><td></td><td><b><font size="2">JAMES JONES, J. P. [Seal.]</font></b></td></tr></tbody></table>
</p></b></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>-56-</p></center><hr>

<center><table border="3" width="65%"><tbody><tr><td bgcolor="ddeeee"><b><font size="3">
T<font size="2">WO </font><b>H<font size="2">UNDRED  </font><b>D<font size="2">OLLARS  </font><b>R<font size="2">EWARD</font><b>,—R</b></b></b></b></font><b><b><b>an away from the subscriber, a certain negro man named Ben, (commonly known by the name of Ben Fox).  Also, one other negro, by the name of Rigdon, who ran away on the 8th of this month.
<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I will give the reward of one hundred dollars for each of the above negroes, to be delivered to me or confined in the jail of Lenoir or Jones county, or <i>for the killing of them, so that I can see them</i>.
<br>November 13, 1836. &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;W. D. COBB.
</b></b></b></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>

<p>Another advertisement, from the Sumpter County (Alabama) <i>Whig</i> will illustrate the methods of slave hunting in other States besides North Carolina:

</p><p></p><center><table border="3" width="66%"><tbody><tr><td bgcolor="ddeeee"><b><font size="4">
NEGRO DOGS.—T</font>he undersigned having bought the entire pack of<b>NEGRO DOGS of the Hay &amp; Allen stock, he now proposes to <i>catch runaway negroes</i>. His charge will be three dollars a day for <i>hunting</i> and fifteen dollars for <i>catching</i> a runaway. He resides three and one-half miles north of Livingston, near the lower Jones' Bluff road.
<br>November 6, 1845.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;WM. GAMBEL.
</b></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>

<p>The New York <i>Commercial-Advertiser</i> of June 8th, 1827, contained the following item of news, not uncommon at that time, as the irresponsibility of <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#tobaccofarmers">slave-holders</a> over the lives of their slaves had hardly been questioned:

</p><blockquote>"HUNTING MEN WITH DOGS.—A negro who had absconded from his master, and for whom a reward of a hundred dollars was offered, has been apprehended and committed to prison in Savannah."
</blockquote></b></b></b></b></font><b><b><b><b>

<p>The editor who states the fact adds, with as much <a href="preventbraindamage.htm#abulia">coolness</a> as though there were no barbarity in the matter, that he did not surrender till he was considerably maimed by the dogs that had been set on him—desperately fighting them, and badly cutting one of them with a sword.
</p><p>The St. Francisville (La.) <i>Chronicle</i> of February 1st, 1839, reports a slave-hunt after this sort:
</p><blockquote>"Two or three days ago a <i>gentleman</i> of this parish, in hunting runaway negroes, came upon a camp of them in the swamp on Cat Island. He succeeded in arresting two of them, but the third made fight. On being shot in the shoulder, he fled to a sluice, where the dogs succeeded in drowning him before <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#rescue">assistance</a> could arrive."</blockquote>
<p>Had "<i><a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?Luke+10:30-37">assistance</a></i> arrived," would it have been tendered to the dogs or their victim? is a question, to
</p><p></p><center>-57-</center><hr>

this day. But calling off the dogs altogether, let the subject be illumined a little farther with lights like this, from the Charleston (S. C.) <i>Courier</i>, in 1825.
<p></p><center><table border="3" width="65%"><tbody><tr><td bgcolor="ddeeee"><b>TWENTY DOLLARS REWARD.—Ran away from the subscriber, on the 14th instant, a negro girl named Molly. She is 16 or 17 years of age slim made, lately BRANDED ON HER LEFT CHEEK THUS, "R," AND A PIECE IS TAKEN OFF HER EAR ON THE SAME SIDE; THE SAME LETTER IS BRANDED ON THE INSIDE OF BOTH HER LEGS.<center><dd>ABNER ROSS, Fairfield District, S. C.</dd></center></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>True, the <i>killing</i> is here omitted, possibly by accident, but if such an atrocity does not involve murder sublimated, what shall be said of this from the Wilmington (N. C.) <i>Advertiser</i> of July 13th, 1838?
</p><p></p><center><table border="3" width="65%"><tbody><tr><td bgcolor="ddeeee"><b>RANAWAY—MY NEGRO MAN. RICHARD.—A reward of twenty-five dollars will be paid for his apprehension, DEAD OR ALIVE! Satisfactory proof will only be required of his being killed. He has with him, in all probability, his wife, Eiza, who ran away from Colonel Thompson, now a resident of Alabama.</b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>

<p>But no more such evidences of the murderous spirit of slavery, can be needed; though the last advertisement suggests an incident in South Carolina, so late as 1844, which is too instructive and assuring not to be given.
</p><p>That "wife, Eliza, who ran away from Colonel Thompson," possibly might have a tale unfolded, whose lightest word would have harrowed up the soul. There were many such tales. A young man in South Carolina was seen walking with a young woman, a slave, to whom it was known he was tenderly attached, and whom, it was farther shown, he married and aided to escape from slavery. That was his crime. He was arrested, tried, and found guilty. Sentence of death was pronounced upon him by Judge J. B. O'Neale, in word and spirit as now reproduced:

<a name="p58jb"></a>
</p><blockquote>"JOHN L. BROWN—It is my duty to announce to you the consequences of the conviction which you heard at Winnsboro', and of the opinion you have just heard read, refusing your two-fold motion in arrest of judgment for a new trial.
<p>"You are to die! To die an ignominious death—the death on the gallows! This announcement is, to</p></blockquote><b>
<p></p><center>-58-</center><hr>

<blockquote>you, I know, most appalling. Little did you dream of it when you stepped into the bar with an air as if you thought it was a fine frolic. But the consequences of <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#rescue">crime</a> are just such as you are realizing. Punishment often comes when it is <a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?Luke+10:30-37">least expected</a>.
<p>"Let me entreat you to take the present opportunity to commence the work of reformation. Time will be furnished you to prepare for the great change just before you. Of your past life I know nothing, except what your trial furnished. That told me that the crime for which you are to suffer was the consequence of a want of attention on your part to the duties of life. The strange woman snared you. She flattered you with her words, and you became her victim. The consequence was, that, led on by a desire to <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#rescue">serve</a> her, you committed the offense of aiding a slave to run away and depart from her master's service; and now, for it you are to die!
</p><p>"You are a young man, and I fear you have been dissolute; and if so, these kindred vices have contributed a full measure to your ruin. Reflect on your past life, and make the only useful devotion of the remnant of your days in preparing for death.
</p><p>"Remember now thy Creator in the days of thy youth, is the language of inspired wisdom. This comes home appropriately to you in this trying moment.
</p><p>"You are young; quite too young to be where you are. If you had remembered your Creator in your past days, you would not now be in a <a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?Luke+10:30-37">felon's place</a>, to receive a felon's judgment. Still, it is not too late to remember your Creator. He calls early, and He calls late. He stretches out the arms of a Father's love to you—to <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#rescue">the vilest sinner</a>—and says: "Come unto me and be saved."
</p><p>"You can perhaps, read. If so, read the Scriptures; read them without note, and without comment; and pray to God for His assistance; and you will be able to say when you pass from prison to execution, as a poor slave said under similar circumstances: 'I am glad my Friday has come.' If you cannot read the Scriptures, the ministers of our <a href="#clergy=atheists">holy religion</a> will be ready to aid you. They will read and</p></blockquote><b>
<p></p><center>-59-</center><hr>

<blockquote>explain to you until you will be able to understand; and understanding, to call upon the only One who can help you and save you—Jesus Christ, the Lamb of God, who taketh away the sin of the world. To Him I commend you. And through Him may you have that opening of the Day-Spring of mercy from on high, which shall bless you here, and crown you as a saint in an everlasting world, forever and ever.
<p>"The sentence of the [pro-slavery] law is that you be taken hence to the place from whence you came last; thence to the jail of Fairfield District; and that there you be closely and securely confined until Friday, the 26th day of April next; on which day, between the hours of ten in the forenoon and two in the afternoon, you will be taken to the place of  public execution, and there be hanged by the neck till your body be dead.  And may God have mercy on your soul!"</p></blockquote>
<p>No event in anti-slavery history up to that time so stirred the two hemispheres as did this frightful sentence of <a name="eviljudge"></a> <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#rescue">Judge O'Neale</a>. Even in the British House of Lords, two illustrious members, Brougham and Denman, gave it pathetic and powerful consideration. One London journal said:</p><blockquote>"The dreadful case of John L. Brown has created throughout Great Britain, a sensation of deepest and most painful character. Addresses to the churches in South Carolina have been extensively signed by the independent churches in England and Scotland."</blockquote>
<p>The Glasgow <i>Argus</i>, among the most important journals of Scotland, twice published the <i>Charge</i> on account of its fearful character, and said of it,</p><blockquote>"we know of nothing more atrocious in the judicial annals of modern times.  *  *  *   And what are we to think of a judge, who in passing sentence for what in our country, our land of Freedom, would be looked upon as <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#rescue">a praiseworthy act</a>, invokes the sacred name of Deity and the Holy Book of Inspiration as lending sanction to the atrocity about to be committed!"</blockquote>
<p></p><center>-60-</center><hr>

<p>But perhaps the most imposing movement in Great Britain, on this terrible perversion of all justice, as well as outrage on all decency, humanity and charity, was a "Memorial addressed to the Churches of Christ in South Carolina, as representing those of other states," signed by more than thirteen hundred ministers and office-holders in the churches and other benevolent associations of London, and other portions of the kingdom, in solemn protest against it. But it need hardly be told, that all the sympathy felt, all the effort made, all the appeals and memorials sent, eloquent, tender, pathetic, devout as many, if not all of them were, seemed almost wholly thrown away on the press, pulpit, and vast majority of the people of the United States, even though South Carolina did yield to foreign pressure at last, and commuted the sentence to fifty lashes on the bare back; and even they were said to have been remitted on condition that the young man quit the state forever.
</p><p><img src="jwhittier.jpg" hspace="8" width="104" height="150" alt="John Greenleaf Whittier" border="1" align="right">


But this account though already extended, would not be complete unless the feelings excited in the hearts of the American Abolitionists, in view of the whole scene, could have utterance.

</p><p>Let then their favorite and faithful poet, [John Greenleaf] Whittier [1807-1892], be their oracle:

</p><p><br></p><center><a name="mem-j-brown"></a><font size="4">ON THE SENTENCE OF JOHN L. BROWN</font>.

<table><tbody><tr><td bgcolor="white"><b>
Ho! thou who seekest late and long
<br>A License from the Holy Book
<br>For brutal lust and hellish wrong,
<br>Man of the Pulpit look!
<br><a name="clergy=atheists"></a>Lift up those <font size="4">cold</font> [unfeeling] and <a href="#atheism"><font size="4" color="red">atheist</font></a> eyes,
<br>This ripe fruit of thy [<a href="#atheism">atheist</a>] teaching see;
<br>And tell us how to heaven will rise
<br>The incense of this sacrifice—
<br>This blossom of the gallows tree!

<p>Search out for slavery's hour of need
<br>Some fitting text of sacred writ;
<br>Give heaven the credit of a deed
<br>Which shames the nether pit.
</p></b></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>-61-</p><hr>

<table width="380"><tbody><tr><td bgcolor="white"><b>
Kneel, smooth blasphemer, unto Him
<br>Whose truth is on thy lips a lie—
<br>Ask that His bright winged cherubim
<br>May bend around that scaffold grim
<br>To guard and bless and sanctify.

<p>Ho! champion of the people's cause—
<br>Suspend thy loud and vain rebuke
<br>Of foreign wrong and Old World's laws—
<br>Man of the Senate, look!
<br>Was <i>this</i> the promise of the free,
<br>The great hope of our early time—
<br>That <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#tobaccofarmers">slavery's poison vine</a> should be
<br>Upborne by Freedom's prayer-nurs'd tree
<br>O'erclustered with such fruits of crime?

</p><p>Send out the summons East and West,
<br>And South and North, let all be there
<br><a href="slaveryillegal.htm#rescue"><img src="jbrown.jpg" width="115" height="138" align="right"></a>Where he who pitied the oppressed
<br>Swings out in sun and air.
<br>Let not a Democratic hand
<br>The grisly hangman's task refuse;
<br>There let each loyal patriot stand,
<br>Awaiting slavery's command,
<br>To twist the rope and draw the noose!

</p><p>But vain is irony—unmeet
<br>Its cold rebuke for deeds which start
<br>In fiery and indignant beat
<br>The pulses of the heart.
<br>Leave studied wit and guarded phrase
<br>For those who think but do not feel—
<br>Let <font size="2">MEN</font><b> speak out in words which raise
<br>Where'er they fall, an answering blaze
<br>Like flints which strike the fire from steel.

</b></p><p><b>Still let a mousing priesthood ply
<br>Their garbled text and gloss of sin,
<br>And make the lettered scroll deny
<br>Its living soul within:
<br>Still let the place-fed, titled knave
<br>Plead robbery's right with purchased lips,
<br>And tell us that <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#4.">our fathers</a> gave
<br>For Freedom's pedestal, a slave,
<br>The frieze and moulding, chains and whips!

</b></p><p><b>But ye who own that Higher Law
<br>Whose tablets in the heart are set,
<br>Speak out in words of power and awe
<br>T<font size="2">HAT </font><b>G<font size="2">OD IS LIVING YET</font><b>!
<br>Breathe forth once more those tones sublime
<br>Which thrilled the burdened prophet's lyre,</b></b></b></p></b></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p></p><center>-62-</center><hr>

<table><tbody><tr><td bgcolor="white"><b>
And in a dark and evil time
<br>Smote down on Israel's fast of crime
<br>And gift of blood, <font size="2">A RAIN OF FIRE!</font><b>

<p>Oh, not for us the graceful lay
<br>To whose soft measures lightly move
<br>The Dryad and the woodland fay,
<br>O'er-locked by mirth and love!
<br>But such a Stern and startling strain
<br>As Britain's hunted bards flung down
<br>From Snowden to the conquered plain,
<br>Where harshly clanked the Saxon chain,
<br>On trampled field and smoking town.

</p><p>By Liberty's dishonored name,
<br>By man's lost hope and failing trust,
<br>By words and deeds which bow with shame
<br>Our foreheads to the dust;
<br>By the exulting Tyrant's sneer,
<br>Borne to us from the Old World's thrones,
<br>And by his victims' griefs who hear,
<br>In sunless mines and dungeons drear,
<br>How Freedom's land her faith disowns!

</p><p>Speak out in <font size="2">ACTS</font><b>, the time for words
<br>Has passed; and <font size="2">DEEDS</font><b> alone suffice;
<br>In the loud clang of meeting swords
<br>The softer music dies!
<br>Act—act in God's name, while ye may!
<br>Smite from the <font size="2">CHURCH</font><b>, her leprous limb!
<br>Throw open to the light of day
<br>The bondman's cell, and break away
<br>The chains the <font size="2">STATE</font><b> has bound on him!

</b></b></b></b></p><p><b><b><b>Ho! every true and living soul,
<br>To Freedom's perilled altar bear
<br>The Freeman's and the Christian's whole
<br>Tongue, pen, and vote, and prayer!
<br>One last, great battle for the right—
<br>One short, sharp struggle to be free!
<br>To do is to <font size="2">SUCCEED</font><b>—our fight
<br>Is waged in Heaven's approving sight;
<br>The smile of God is Victory."
</b></b></b></b></p></b></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>

<p></p><center><table border="1&quot;"><tbody><tr><td bgcolor="ddeeeee"><b><center><font face="Arial" size="2">Ed Note: <a href="patton1846.htm#poemagainstvileclergy">Rev. Wm. Patton quoted a related poem by John G. Whittier</a>.
<hr>See also, a <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#rescue">legal history analysis of slave-rescue action of the type Brown had attempted</a>,
<hr>Edward C. Rogers also linked slavers and atheism, at pp <a href="rogersuos.htm#p5-atheism">5</a> and <a href="rogersuos.htm#p33-atheists">33</a>.
<hr>Rev. Wm. Goodell's <a href="goodellsaas.htm#anti-atheist-clergy">support of this concept (identifying pro-slavery clergy as atheists)</a>
<hr>and a <a href="patton1846.htm#listvileclergy">list of vile clergymen</a>, corrupt supporters of the sin of slavery.</font></center></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>

<p>Severity of punishments inflicted on slaves short of death, were often a thousand times more cruel than death by the halter; not unfrequently terminating in death, though only by whipping. But hanging was not always severe enough, <a name="judicialmurder"></a>as witness a law of Maryland, enacted in 1729:
</p><p>"The slave shall first have the
</p><p></p><center>-63-</center><hr>

<blockquote>right hand cut off, then be hanged in the usual manner; the head be severed from the body, the body divided into four quarters, and the head and quarters be set up in the most public places of the county where such act was committed."</blockquote>And this horrible barbarity could be inflicted by a simple justice's court.
<p>But it may be said this legislation was before the foundations of this republic were laid. That is true. But in the year 1836, in the city of St. Louis, Missouri, an act was perpetrated, of which the following was the accepted newspaper account, on the spot and over the country:
</p><blockquote>On the 28th of April, 1836, in the city of St. Louis, a black man named Mcintosh, who [in attempting <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#rescue">self-rescue</a> as per precedents] had stabbed an [<a href="feeasm1851.htm#p10-extortion">extortioner</a>, i.e., felony-committing] officer who had arrested him,
<ul><a name="p64-burning-alive"></a><li>was seized by the multitude [lynch mob],
<p></p></li><li>fastened to a tree in the midst of the city,
<p></p></li><li>wood piled around him, in open day, and in the presence of an immense throng of citizens,
<p></p></li><li>he was burned to death.</li></ul> </blockquote>

<p>The Alton <i>Telegraph</i> thus describes a part of the scene:
</p><blockquote>"All was silent as death while the executioners were piling the wood around the victim. He said not a word till he felt that the flames had seized him. He then uttered an awful howl, attempting to sing and pray, then hung his head and suffered in silence.
<p>"After the flames had surrounded their prey, his eyes burned out of his head, and his mouth apparently parched to a cinder, <a href="kevorkian.htm#tortureandkill">some one in the crowd</a> more compassionate than the rest, proposed to end his misery by shooting him.
</p><p>"But it was replied that he was already out of his pain. "No, no," cried the wretch, "I am not. I am suffering as much as ever. Shoot me! Shoot me!"
</p><p>"No," exclaimed <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#tobaccofarmers">one of the fiends</a> standing by the roasting sacrifice, " no, he shall not be shot. I would sooner slack the fire if that would increase his misery!"</p></blockquote>
<p>A St. Louis correspondent of a New York paper sent an account of the diabolical deed, of which this is an excerpt:
</p><p></p><center>-64-</center><hr>

<blockquote><b>"The shrieks and groans of the victim were loud and piercing, and to observe one limb after another drop into the fire, was awful indeed. In dying, he was about fifteen minutes.
<p>"I visited the place this morning and saw the body, or the remains of it, burned to a crump. The legs and arms were gone, and only a part of the head and body was remaining."</p></b></blockquote><b><b>
<p>A subsequent Judicial decision by judge Luke E. Lawless, of the Circuit Court of Missouri, made at a session of court in St. Louis, was, that as the burning of Mcintosh was the act, directly or indirectly, by countenance of a majority of the citizens, it is a case which transcends the jurisdiction of the grand jury!
</p><p>And so the dreadful sacrament was sanctified and solemnized by high judicial decision. And as such atrocities were common while slavery lasted, why need the law of Maryland be shorn of its odium and terror in the popular apprehension, only because it was older than the <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#declaration">Declaration of American Independence</a>?
</p><p>Assuming that nations are not better than their laws, or that laws are never made till needed, what shall be said of legislation like this? A law of North Carolina provided that:
</p><blockquote>If any person shall wilfully kill his own slave, or of any other person, every such offender shall, on conviction, forfeit and pay the sum of seven hundred pounds, and shall forever be rendered incapable of holding or exercising any office.</blockquote><b>
<p>And this law was not repealed till the year 1821, if ever. Another section of the same act provided:
</p><blockquote>If any person shall, in sudden heat of passion, or by undue correction, kill his own slave, or the slave of any other person, he shall forfeit the sum of three hundred and fifty pounds.</blockquote>
<p>A still further provision of the same act read thus:
</p><blockquote>If any person shall wilfully cut out the tongue, put out the eye, castrate, or cruelly scald or burn any</blockquote>
<p></p><center>-65-</center><hr>


<blockquote>slave, or deprive any slave of any limb or member, or shall inflict any other cruel punishment, other than by whipping or beating with a switch, horse-whip or cow-skin, or by putting on irons, or imprisoning such slave, such person, for every such offence, shall forfeit and pay one hundred pounds.</blockquote>
<p>Judge Stroud, in his carefully prepared "<i>Sketch of Laws Relating to Slavery</i>," says in his latest edition, (1856): "This, <i>so far as I can learn</i>, has been suffered to disgrace the statute book to the present hour. Amid all the mutations which Christianity has effected within the last century, she has not been able to conquer the spirit which dictated this law."
</p><p>And not to speak of the shameful outrage, so denounced in <a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?Deuteronomy+23:1">Deuteronomy, xxiii, i</a>, what must be thought of the decency, humanity, not to say religion, of a people that enacts, supports, sanctifies a law which beats without limit, without mercy, with horse-whip, cowskin or other missile, a human being, man, woman, child, unrebuked, unless the last stroke should produce immediate death?
</p><p>With one more well authenticated fact and one other witness, and he none other than Thomas Jefferson himself, the question as to the character of slavery shall be submitted to readers, to history, to posterity. The outrage to be described was witnessed by John James Appleton, Esq., whom Hon. David Lee Child and his illustrious wife, Mrs. Lydia Maria Child, endorse as "a gentleman of high attainments and accomplishments," a secretary of legation at Rio Janeiro, Madrid and the Hague, commissioner at Naples and charge d'affaires at Stockholm.   Mr. Appleton was present at the burial of a female slave in Mississippi, who had been whipped to death by her master, for being gone longer on an errand than was thought necessary. She protested under the terrible
</p><p></p><center>-66-</center><hr>

torture that she was ill and had to rest in the fields. To complete the climax of horror, she was delivered of a dead child while undergoing the punishment!! Is it strange that she had to rest by the way? But we will hasten to our last witness.
<p>To-day as I write, the Democratic party, party of Thomas Jefferson, is celebrating here in Massachusetts, a political success, almost unexampled under the circumstances, in state elections, since the party was first inaugurated. The tribes of Israel never claimed Abraham as their father with more devout pride and filial reverence, than have the Democrats of this nation Thomas Jefferson as theirs, since their party first learned to lisp his name.
</p><p>And those tribes crying, "Crucify Him, crucify Him," in the court-room of Pilate, or mocking their victim as he climbed Mount Calvary, bearing his cross in sweating agony, did not more dishonor their patriarchal father and founder than did the Democratic party and their Whig accomplices on the plains of Texas, <a name="warmexico"></a>murdering the Mexicans in a bloody war to reinstate slavery where the Mexican government, with its <a href="catholicsvslavery.htm">Roman Catholic religion</a>, had not many years before, abolished it, as all humanity hoped, forever. That was almost forty years ago. Undoubtedly, devotion to slavery sent the old Whig party to a scarcely too early grave. Worship of the same unclean and bloody Moloch, stove down democratic rule, from the kindled wrath of the Infinite Justice around Fort Sumter, until the victories won yesterday in so many States of this Union, and proudly celebrated to-day, give sign almost unmistakable, of its probable return at the next presidential election.
</p><p>And now the next and last witness as to the whole quality and character of slavery, even as he saw it
</p><p></p><center>-67-</center><hr>

and himself embraced it, is the patriarchal American Democrat, Thomas Jefferson [1743-1826] himself.

<p><img src="http://www.whitehouse.gov/history/presidents/images/tj3.gif" align="right" width="176" height="261" hspace="4" alt="Thomas Jefferson" border="1">
His memorable "<i>Notes on the State of Virginia</i>,"  so often cited in the past, so greatly disregarded while slavery continued, were revised and published in 1787, when the problem of slavery was shaking the new republic to its foundation.
</p><p>The section relating to slavery contains so many general observations on human relations and obligations, individual as well as collective, social as well as civil and governmental, with a profoundly reverent recognition of higher authority than any man-made institutions, or constitutions, that it surely is not too much to declare that a return of the Democratic party to power will be a blessing or scourge and curse, exactly in proportion as it shall follow, or reject the doctrines and counsels of its justly venerated founder and progenitor, as laid down in the passage from his "<i>Notes on the State of Virginia</i>," here reproduced:
</p><p><br></p><center><table width="520" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b><a name="notes-excerpt"></a>"There must doubtless be an unhappy influence on the manners of our people, produced by the existence of <i>Slavery</i> among us. The whole commerce between master and slave is a perpetual exercise of the most boisterous passions, the most unremitting despotism on the one part, and degrading submission on the other.
<p><a name="adult-example"></a>"Our children see this and learn to imitate it; for man is an imitative animal. This quality in him is the germ of all education. From his cradle to his grave, he is learning to do what he sees others do. If a parent could find no motive, either in his philanthropy or his self-love, for restraining the intemperance of passion toward his slave, it should always be a sufficient one that his child is present. But generally it is not sufficient. The parent storms, the child looks on, catches the lineaments of wrath, puts on the same airs in the circle of smaller slaves, gives a loose to the worst of passions, and thus nursed and educated, and daily exercised in tyranny, cannot but be stamped by</p></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p></p><center>-68-</center><hr>

<center><table width="520" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>it with odious peculiarities.  The man must be a prodigy who can retain his manners and morals undepraved by such circumstances.
<p>"And with what execration should the statesman be loaded, who, permitting one-half the citizens thus to trample on the rights of the other, transforms those into despots, and these into enemies, destroys the morals of one part and the <i>amor patriæ</i> of the other! For if a slave can have a country in this world, it must be any other in preference to that in which he is born to live and labor for another; in which he must lock up the faculties of his nature; contribute, as far as depends on his individual endeavors, to the evanishment of the human race, or entail his own miserable condition on the endless generations proceeding from him.
</p><p>"With the morals of the people, their industry also is destroyed. For in a warm climate no man will labor for himself who can make another labor for him. This is so true, that of the proprietors of slaves, a very small proportion indeed are ever seen to labor. And can the liberties of a nation be thought secure when we have removed their only firm basis-a conviction in the minds of the people that these liberties are the gift of God? That they are not to be violated but with his wrath?
</p><p>"Indeed, I tremble for my country when I reflect that God is just: that his justice cannot sleep forever: that considering numbers, and natural means only, a revolution of the wheel of Fortune, an exchange of situation is among possible events: that it may become probable by supernatural interference! The Almighty has no attribute which can take sides with us in such a contest!
</p><p>"But it is impossible to be temperate and pursue this subject through the various considerations of policy, of morals, of history, natural and civil. We must be contented to hope they will force their way into every mind. I think a change already perceptible since the origin of the present <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#Declaration">revolution</a>. The spirit of the master is abating; that of the slave rising from the dust; his condition molifying; the way I hope preparing, under the auspices of heaven, for a total</p></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p></p><center>-69-</center><hr>

<center><table width="520" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>emancipation, And that this is disposed, in the order of events, to be with the consent of the masters, rather than by their extirpation."</b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>Such was American slavery. Jefferson proved its historian as well as prophet, to wondrous extent.  Happy for the nation, had it heeded his <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#declaration">wise and timely counsels</a>. Happy for it would it even now learn to regard them.
</p><p>When, before or since our slave system, did governments ever punish with death for seventy offences, and then forbid, under penalties almost as severe as death, to teach one of the victims of such tyranny to read one law of man or God, in any book, the Bible not excepted? It may have been, But when, or where? What but cold-blooded murder must such governing have been! To rid the land of such a <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#tobaccofarmers">plague</a>, no wonder it required an army on our side only, of more than two million seven hundred thousand men, half a million of whom never returned!
</p><p>And then, as a crowning, sealing sacrifice, an idolized president [<font face="Arial" size="0">Ed. Note: <a href="lincolnpeoria.htm">Abraham Lincoln</a></font>] massacred, murdered, and his tall form stretched across their premature graves, while not this nation only, but foreign peoples stood aghast! All this, not to speak of moneyed cost and loss; nor counting the sighs and tears, bereavements and mournings of mothers, sisters, widows and orphans! All this, not reckoning moral and spiritual, as well as financial impoverishment and desolation, not to be restored perhaps till our third and fourth generations! Such was part of the price paid to redeem the land from its uncommon curse.
</p><p>Men called the war of sword and bayonet, <i>Rebellion</i>.  It might have been <a href="slaveryillegal.htm">rebellion</a> on the part of slavery and the South. But to the North it was <i>Retribution</i>.   The. South claimed as property, the slave. But the North, by the terms of the Federal
</p><p></p><center>-70-</center><hr>

Union, held him pinned down to the earth as with the point of the bayonet. From the <a href="kevorkian.htm#tortureandkill">torture-chambers</a> of the imprisoned slave our guilt ascended, by silent but sure evaporation, until it hung in threatening clouds over all the sky, waiting the dread hour when the <a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?Exodus+21:16">Infinite Patience</a> could endure it no longer!
<p>At last the command was given, and the tempest and thunder shook the very heavens, saying to the North, "Give up;" to the South, "Keep not back."  No lightning-rod shielded either; and <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#tobaccofarmers">Slavery</a>, with all its reeking, shrieking altars, and ghastly paraphernalia of whips, fetters, bloodhounds and red-hot branding irons, was swept away in cataclysms of blood and fire!
</p><p></p><center>-71-</center><hr>

<center><a name="p72"></a>CHAPTER IV.
<p>ANTI-SLAVERY — WHAT IT WAS NOT.
<br>AND WHAT IT WAS.
</p></center>
<p>Such account could slavery give of itself, "Peculiar Institution " it was often called. But it was not peculiar to the southern states. Fortunes were made by the African slave trade, even in little Rhode Island. The history of slavery and slave trading in Massachusetts is one of the most surprising volumes ever issued by the American press. New Hampshire held slaves.
</p><p><img src="gwashington.bmp" width="72" height="76" alt="George Washington" border="0" hspace="3" align="right">General Washington himself while President of the United States, hunted a slave woman and her child all the way into that then remote state.
</p><p>Vermont, had a fugitive slave case in 1808. <a name="judge-deny-it">But the brave </a><a href="shawuos.htm#judge-deny-it">Judge Harrington</a> stunned the remorseless claimant with his decision that "nothing less than a bill of sale from the Almighty could establish ownership" in his victim.

</p><p></p><center><table border="3" bgcolor="fffffa"><tbody><tr><td><b>Ed. Note:

<li>Senator Charles Sumner in 1860 cited both <a href="sumnerbarbarism.htm#p132">this concept</a> and <a href="sumnerbarbarism.htm#p219-harrington">this case</a>.
</li><li>Edward C. Rogers in 1855 traced back to <a href="rogersuos.htm#p93-source">American-Revolution-era concepts</a>.
</li><li>Rev. John Rankin <a href="rankin1823.htm#only-creator">cited parental context</a>.</li></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>

<p>And he [Judge Harrington], too, returned home despoiled and shamed.

</p><p>Slavery was the sin and crime of north as well as south. It was sustained by the government, it was sanctified by almost the whole religion of the nation. I have read that even the Quakers gravely considered the question, not whether it was right to hold slaves, but whether it was proper to brand them with red hot marking irons. To the credit of that sect, however, it should be told that it was among the first, <a href="catholicsvslavery.htm">if not the very first</a>, to cast the accursed thing forever out of its fellowship.

</p><p>Three clauses in the <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#4.">federal constitution</a> were so interpreted as to brand the whole nation as slave-holders, slave-hunters and slave-traders; and one of
</p><p></p><center>-72-</center><hr>

those clauses was in two words, "suppress insurrections." And another was in this <a href="spooneruos.htm#p279">apparently innocent, inoffensive</a> period:
<blockquote>"No person held to service or labor in one state under the laws thereof, escaping into another, shall, in consequence of any law or regulation therein, be discharged from such service or labor; but shall be delivered up on claim of the party to whom such service or labor may be due."</blockquote>

<p><a name="p73b"></a><img src="gwashington.bmp" width="72" height="76" alt="George Washington" border="0" hspace="3" align="right">And under that <a href="tiffanyuos.htm#p7">guarantee</a>, which, as president, he was solemnly sworn to execute, did George Washington himself pursue a slave mother [Ona Maria Judge] and her child from the Potomac to the Piscatauqua as remorselessly as though they had been a sheep and her lamb. Fortunately, however, for the victims, they escaped and lived and died in the old Granite State.

</p><p></p><center><table width="560" bordercolor="tan" border="4" bgcolor="ccffff"><tbody><tr><td><b>Ed. Note: For details, see note in Alvan Stewart, <i>Legal Argument</i> (1845), <a href="stewartuos.htm#p50-gwe">p 50</a>, citing   <a href="http://www.american.edu/sis/faculty/clusane.cfm">Prof</a>. <a href="http://clarencelusane.blogspot.com/">Clarence</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clarence_Lusane">Lusane</a>, Ph.D., <i><a href="http://www.citylights.com/book/?GCOI=87286100744980&amp;fa=author&amp;person_id=9404">Black History in the White House</a></i> (San Francisco: City Lights Books, 2011), Chapter 1, pp 35-47.</b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>

<p>Our African slave trade was a <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#6.">piracy</a> that paled all ordinary buccaneering into innocence. That traffic, with all its nameless terrors and tortures, was secured to the United States and positively protected by this <a href="spooneruos.htm">specious and apparently inoffensive phrase</a> in the ninth section of Article I in the <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#4.">federal constitution</a>:
</p><blockquote>"The migration or importation of such persons as any of the states now existing shall think proper to admit, shall not be prohibited by the Congress prior to the year one thousand eight hundred and eight, but a tax or duty may be imposed on such importations, not exceeding ten dollars for each person."</blockquote>
<p>And Mr. Madison, afterwards president, declared, and it is part of our <a href="spooneruos.htm#p213r2">history</a>, that "the southern states <a href="spooneruos.htm#p124">would not have entered the union</a> without the temporary permission of that trade."
</p><p>The first <a href="spooneruos.htm#p279">fugitive slave law</a> was enacted in 1793. But as anti-slavery sentiment increased, through the faithful and persistent labors of the uncompromising Abolitionists, "<a href="http://www.freedomcenter.org">underground railroads</a>," as they
</p><p></p><center>-73-</center><hr>

were called, multiplied, and <a href="#judge-deny-it">Judge Harrington's</a> decisions became more frequent.
<p><img src="flagcanada.gif" align="right" hspace="3">Underground railroads were only lines of travel through the northern states to Canada, over which, under cover of night, great numbers of slaves were conveyed, sometimes in whole families; one anti-slavery man hurrying them from his town to the next, or farther, if necessary, and then another taking them in charge, and so on till they were safely landed in Canada, beyond reach of further pursuit or danger.
</p><p><img src="hbstowe.jpg" width="177" height="225" alt="Harriet Beecher Stowe" align="left">"<i><a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20080913231136/http://etext.lib.virginia.edu/toc/modeng/public/StoCabi.html">Uncle Tom's Cabin</a></i>" [by <a href="keytom.htm">Harriet Beecher Stowe</a>] has no more interesting chapter than that in which "Senator Bird's" adventure is described with his night express train over that memorable but dark and dangerous highway out of democratic despotism to <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#2.">freedom in a land of kings and queens</a>. And large numbers escaped with greater security, as their friends multiplied along the way, by their own unaided efforts.


</p><p><a name="p74-fug-sl-law">So [<font color="brown">Ed. Note: pursuant to Southern slaver reaction to escapes to freedom, like when the East German Communists built the Berlin Wall in 1961, to keep people in</font><b>] another and severer fugitive law was demanded, and </b></a><b><a href="abolitionists.htm#changein1850">in 1850 enacted</a>.

</b></p><p><b>That law, in the first place,
</b></p><p></p><blockquote><ul><li><b>made every inch of our country, and the deck of every vessel, on sea, lake or river, hunting ground for slave-holder and kidnapper.
<p></p></b></li><li><b>And whoever refused to aid in the bloody, brutal business of chasing, seizing and holding the human prey, when called into the service, or harbored or concealed the victims so that they escaped, was punished "by fine not exceeding six thousand dollars, and imprisonment not exceeding six months."<p></p></b></li><li><b>And, moreover, could be then held in an action for damages to the slave claimant, for one thousand dollars for every slave lost through refusal to obey that most shameful as well as unrighteous and inhuman edict.</b></li></ul></blockquote><b>
<p>And many of the best families in the land were beggared only for religiously observing the Golden Rule and remembering and-
</p><p></p><center>-74-</center><hr>

regarding them who were in bonds as bound with them. [<a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?Hebrews+13:3">Heb. 13:3</a>].
<p>As early as the year 1840 [<font color="brown">Ed. Note: meaning, <a href="goodellsaas.htm#slaveryunconstitutional">next election after anti-slavery newspapers</a> raised the issue</font>], efforts began to be made by some anti-slavery men, who had faith or hope in political action against slavery, to change the interpretations of the constitution and decisions of the Supreme Court so as to make not only the clauses just now cited, but <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#4.">the whole instrument a proclamation and protection of universal liberty</a>. Foremost among these men was <a name="gerrit-smith-uos"></a>Mr. Gerrit Smith [1797-1874], of New York.

</p><p></p><center>
<a name="p75-uos-list"></a>
<table border="3"><tbody><tr><td bgcolor="fffffa"><b>Ed. Note: And others,
<li><a href="goodellsaas.htm#slaveryunconstitutional">Samuel May</a> (1836)
</li><li><a href="goodellsaas.htm#nprogersp476">Nathaniel Rogers</a> (1837)
</li><li><a href="mellenuos.htm">George Mellen</a> (1841)
</li><li><a href="stewartuos.htm">Alvan Stewart</a> (1845)
</li><li><a href="spooneruos.htm">Lysander Spooner</a> (1845)
</li><li><a href="shawuos.htm">Benjamin Shaw</a> (1846)
</li><li><a href="slaveryillegal.htm#howard-james-uos">Howard James</a> (1849)
</li><li><a href="tiffanyuos.htm">Joel Tiffany</a> (1849)
</li><li><a href="goodellsaas.htm">William Goodell</a> (1852)
</li><li><a name="p75-lincoln"></a><a href="lincolnpeoria.htm#alreadyfreebycommonlaw">Abraham Lincoln</a> (1854)
</li><li><a href="douglassuos.htm">Frederick Douglass</a> (1860),
<br><dd>as per list, <i><a href="#p29-uos-list">supra</a></i><a href="#p29-uos-list">, p 29</a>.
</dd></li></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>

<p>A third political party was inaugurated, and <a href="abolitionists.htm#birneybooks">James G. Birney</a>, whose name has already had honorable mention <a href="#p53-query">in these pages</a>, was the first nominated anti-slavery candidate for the presidency, and whose first anti-slavery works, as a repentant slave-holder, entitled him to such distinction. But his name was withdrawn after his first vote was given in 1844, and John P. Hale of New Hampshire, succeeded him. He also was superseded in the candidacy for one who undoubtedly might control a larger vote, Martin Van Buren [1782-1862], but whose anti-slavery reputation was surely of most questionable character.
