</p><p>"These books are on the shelves of our depositories. Why should they remain there, when now they may be placed in the hands of the population at Matamoras, Monterey, Tampico, Vera Cruz, Jalapa, Perote, Puebla, etc., etc.?
</p><p>"Will those whose obligations bind them to this circulation, answer this question?
</p><p><i>The sword has opened the way</i>.
</p><p>"Our officers and soldiers themselves need all the kindly influences we can exert on them. They will gratefully receive these publications, and bless their benefactors.
</p><p>"Shall we withhold them from the men <i>who fight the</i> [<a href="slaveryillegal.htm#expandslaverygoal">pro-slavery expansion</a>] <i>battles of the country? Many of the officers and soldiers, particularly among the volunteers, are church members</i>, and will rejoice in such an enterprise.
</p><p>"Colporteurs [<a href="http://www.bartleby.com/61/30/C0493000.html">preachers</a>] can be found on the ground. Discharged volunteers will remain, and instead of shooting balls, will love to do good, and communicate to the millions perishing around them, the word of life.
</p><p>"What is my duty as an AMERICAN CHRISTIAN?
</p><p>"Let the hundreds of thousands of Christian freemen in our land answer that question.
</p><p>"<i>If Captain Bragg gave "a little more grape"</i> [cannon fire at Mexicans defending their country from U.S. agression] <i>and turned</i> [caused] <i>the victory, why may not the sons of peace and righteousness</i> follow up that [military slaughter] victory, with all</p></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p></p><center>-384-</center><hr>

<center><table width="498" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>those missiles and weapons which are mighty through God to the pulling down of strongholds? [<a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?2Corinthians+10:4">2 Cor. 10:4</a>]. It must be done.
<p>"<i>It is the great movement of the present century</i>. Who will lead the advance?
<br></p><center><dd></dd><dd></dd><dd></dd><dd>J. C. S.</dd></center></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>And this one more, from the Nashville, Tennessee, <i>Union</i>:
</p><p></p><center><table width="498" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>"At a <i>Missionary</i> meeting, held in the Methodist church, on Monday night, funds were raised for making General [Zachary] Taylor, Colonel Campbell, Colonel Anderson, Captain Cheatham, and Captain Foster, life-members of the Conference Missionary society. These compliments will be duly appreciated by the brave officers, who are <i>winning laurels</i> on the field of battle."</b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>So did the government and the governing part and power of the <a href="patton1846.htm#listvileclergy">vile</a>, <a href="abolitionists.htm#demonized-south">demonized</a>, <a href="pillsburypacts.htm#p377-ed-note">heathen-while-professing-"Christian</a>" church, cooperate in <i>fighting and robbing</i> to extend, as well as support slavery.
</p><p></p><center>-385-</center><hr>

<a name="p386"></a><center>CHAPTER XV.
<p>ACTS OF PRO-SLAVERY APOSTLES—GENERAL ASSEMBLY
<br>OF THE PRESBYTERIAN CHURCH—AMERICAN BOARD
<br>OF COMMISSIONERS FOR FOREIGN MISSIONS—THE
<br>BAPTIST CHURCH—METHODIST EPISCOPAL CHURCH—
<br>PROTESTANT EPISCOPAL CHURCH—CAMPBELLITES—
<br>AMERICAN BIBLE AND TRACT SOCIETIES—
<br>FUGITIVE SLAVE LAW.</p></center>
<p>A long chapter this may be, though it is last but one, and that one, readers may be glad to know, will not be long. The charges against the church and clergy may be sweeping and severe. All that is now proposed is to submit their own recorded, printed, published testimony in support of them. In scripture phrase, "By their own words shall they be justified, or condemned." [<a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?Matthew+12:37">Matt. 12:37</a>]
</p><p>For convenience, the great representative ecclesiastical bodies will be considered separately, beginning with the General Assembly of the Presbyterian church. Notwithstanding its powerful testimony against sla- very, so late as 1818, as has been shown, it grew to be one of the boldest blasphemers against the holy spirit of freedom the world has produced. And the <i>New-school</i> assembly, after the memorable separation into old and new schools, became quite as unscrupulous as the other. Though slavery had little or nothing to do in dividing the body, the new school was much the strongest in the northern states.
</p><p>In the new-school general assembly, in 1840, a motion was made, by a member, on the subject of slavery, when Rev. Dr. Cox, of Brooklyn, immediately
</p><p></p><center>-386-</center><hr>

moved its indefinite postponement. On the motion being carried, he exultingly exclaimed: "Our Vesuvius is capped safely for three years," that being the time for the next meeting. And Judge Birney assures us that Dr. Cox was at the first an abolitionist.
<p>The clergy of the old-school were even more demonstrative in opposition to anything like hostility to the "peculiar institution." When the Richmond ministers held a meeting, expressly to wash their hands clean before all the world, of any anti-slavery stain, Dr. William L. Plummer was absent; but on his return, he made haste to assure his acceptance and approval of the action taken, by a letter to the committee of correspondence, from which these are extracts:
</p><p></p><center><table width="520" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b><a name="lynchabols"></a>"I have carefully watched this matter from its earliest existence, and everything I have seen or heard of its character, both from its patrons and its enemies, has confirmed me beyond repentance, in the belief, that, let the character of abolitionists be what it may in the sight of the judge of all the earth, this is the most meddlesome, impudent, reckless, fierce, and wicked excitement I ever saw.
<p>"If abolitionists will set the country in a blaze, it is but fair that they should receive the first warming at the fire.
</p><p>"Abolitionists are like infidels, wholly unaddicted to martyrdom for opinion's sake. Let them understand that <i>they will be caught</i> [lynched] if they come among us, and they will take good heed to keep out of our way. There is not one man among them who has any more idea of shedding his blood in this cause than he has of making war on the grand Turk."</p></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>To these instances of clerical devotion to the worship of the bloody idol, Judge Birney adds this; a letter from a reverend divine, announcing his inten-
</p><p></p><center>-387-</center><hr>

<p></p><center><img src="undercon.gif" width="128" height="149"><br>(pp 388-403)</center><p>


</p><p>the board reported its work <i>done</i> in the Choctaw nation, and in 1860, in the Cherokee, and gave its legitimate, logical reason:

</p><blockquote>"The Cherokees are a Christian people."    *    *    *
<p>" *    *    *   "The committee have arrived at the conclusion that it is time to discontinue its expenditures among the Cherokees. To prevent all misapprehension, it should be stated at the outset: First, <i>that this is not owing to the relations of our work among those Indians to the system of slavery</i>."</p></blockquote>
The Choctaw mission was similarly closed, in 1859.

<p>O, no! "Slavery had nothing to do with it!" The board found slavery among the Indians in 1817, accepted it as of divine appointment, and compelled its missionaries to accept it, and they did; as did the churches and pulpits that sustained them. When the anti-slavery agitation reached to the churches, and protests were sent up against a slave-holding religion, supported at home, and sent abroad to the heathen, the Board refused to interfere.

</p><p><a name="censor-sandwich-missionaries"></a>And once when the Sandwich Island missionaries sent home a most powerful remonstrance against slave-holding in the churches as a hindrance to their missionary work as well as false to the true Christian faith, the Board <i>suppressed their testimony</i>, and by solemn resolution duly adopted, recorded and published, virtually imposed silence on the subject, at every missionary station under its patronage. When the interest on the subject began to threaten loss to the treasury, then the Board by its Secretaries attempted, by argument to justify slavery as supported by scripture; by patriarchal practice and apostolic approval, the very chiefest apostle [Paul] actually, as was [<a href="feeasm1851.htm#philemon">falsely</a>] claimed, voluntarily restoring a runaway slave to his owner. From 1817 to 1860, more than forty years, did the Board conduct the religious and moral

</p><p></p><center>-404-</center><hr>


education of those Indian tribes, gathering them into churches: masters and slaves alike, with this law in full force:
<blockquote>"No slave, or child of a slave, is to be taught to read or write, in or at any school, by any one connected in any capacity therewith, on pain of dismissal and expulsion from the nation."</blockquote>
<p>And now one thought more, and the Board and all its work shall be discharged from further consideration.
</p><p>In 1860 the Cherokees, and in 1859 the Choctaws, were graduated by the Board from paganism to Christianity with full credentials, as no longer in the darkness of the heathen world. In 1861, the war of Rebellion set the country on fire. The Indian tribes were early awake to the situation. The New York <i>Evangelist</i>, on the 21st of March of the same year, 1861, said: "the Cherokee, Choctaw, and other Indian tribes of the south-west, nearly all of them slave-holders, are evidently under the influence of the secessionists. The principal Choctaw chief hastened to convene the local legislature  *    *    *   and recommended a general council of the Chickasaws, Creeks, Seminoles and Choctaws to be held at a central point for the purpose of adopting some line of policy necessary to their security."
</p><p>In August following, the New York <i>Journal of Commerce</i> announced that: "The Choctaws, Creeks, Seminoles and Chickasaws have given their adherence to the Confederates, and probably the Cherokees are divided on the question."
</p><p>Of the rest, we know enough. How well and truly was it said at the opening of the war of Rebellion, "in the forty-two years of the maintenance of the Cherokee and Choctaw missions, by the American Board,
</p><p></p><center>-405-</center><hr>


they have <i>connived</i> at slavery, avoiding, by various dishonorable and dishonest means and contrivances, the hard duty of reformation. Now they go a step further, spontaneously and publicly vouching for slave-holding churches as <i>christian</i> churches, and for a nation upholding the worst form of slavery, as "<i>a Christian people</i>."
<p>And between four and five thousand armed Indian warriors, led by an able Boston born general, the tallest, handsomest officer in the rebel army, at the battle of Pea Ridge, fighting in a war waged by slave-holders and waged wholly for slavery, and nothing else, was a spectacle worthy the American Board of Commissioners for Foreign Missions!
</p><p><a name="p406baptist"></a></p><center><font size="2">THE BAPTIST CHURCH.</font><b></b></center><b>
<p>Or if we turn to the Baptist denomination with its vast proportion in the slave states, the record will not improve; and in that again we can learn its spirit and position through its great national Foreign Missionary Association known in early anti-slavery days as <i>The Baptist Triennial Convention</i>. The harmony in it seems never to have been disturbed by the slavery problem till broken by the tocsin of the abolitionists. For so late as the year 1834, Rev. Dr. Bolles, of Boston, one of its Secretaries of correspondence in an official paper, said:
</p><blockquote>"There is a pleasing degree of union among the multiplying thousands of Baptists throughout the land. *    *    *    Our southern brethren are generally slave-holders, both ministers and people." [See <a href="#p326-parody">Parody</a>].</blockquote>

<p>And another Boston Baptist doctor of divinity, Rev. Daniel Sharp, wrote under date January 21st, 1840:
</p><blockquote>"There were undoubtedly both slave-holders and slaves in the primitive churches; I therefore for one, do not feel myself at liberty to make conditions of commun-</blockquote>
<p></p><center>-406-</center><hr>


<blockquote>ion which neither Christ nor his apostles made. I do not feel myself wiser nor better than were they;    * *  *    and I believe that a majority of the wisest and best men at the north hold to these sentiments."
[<font face="Arial" size="2">Ed. Note: Rebutted by Rev. John Fee, in <i>An Anti-Slavery Manual</i>, <a href="feeasm1851.htm#apostles'silence">pages 110-121</a></font>]</blockquote>

<p>In 1841, at the Triennial convention and by appointment, a slave-holder presided, another slave-holder performed the devotional exercises, and a third slave-holder preached the Triennial sermon; and the Rev. Elon Galusha, of New York, an earnest, outspoken anti-slavery man, was removed from the Board of Management, partly, or principally, from demands like this of the Camden, South Carolina Baptist church:
</p><p></p><center><table border="1" width="65%"><tbody><tr><td bgcolor="ddeeee"><b>
<i>Resolved</i>, That we view with contempt, the advice, opinions, menaces and declarations of Elon Galusha and his coadjuutors contained in their address to southern Baptists.
<p><i>Resolved</i>, That we recommend to our associations to use their influence to have Elon Galusha expelled from his office as Vice-President of the Board of Foreign Missions.
</p><p><font size="4">The fifth, sixth and seventh, ran thus:</font>
</p><p><i>Resolved</i>, That we extend to northern Baptists who are opposed to the abolitionists, our warmest affection and fraternal regard. They will ever have an interest in our prayers.
</p><p><i>Resolved</i>, That the address of Elon Galusha be returned to him, with request that he will never again insult us with an address of any kind.
</p><p><i>Resolved</i>, That these proceedings be published in the <i>Christian Index, The Biblical Recorder, Religious Herald, New York Baptist Advocate, and Camden Journal</i>.
<table><tbody><tr><td width="57%"></td><td><b>C. M. BLEEKER, <i>Chairman</i>,
</b></td></tr><tr><td></td><td><b>E. G. ROBINSON, <i>Secretary</i>.</b></td></tr></tbody></table>
</p></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>And as already told, Mr. Galusha was removed. All the proceedings appeared to have been in keeping
</p><p></p><center>-407-</center><hr>


with such expulsion; for the meeting closed with the sacramental supper and singing:
<p></p><center>"Lo, what an entertaining sight
<br>Are brethren who agree!"</center>
<p>A member writing to the <i>Biblical Recorder and Southern Watchman</i>, thus rejoices:
</p><blockquote>"Our meeting was truly delightful. The spirit of the gospel prevailed, and gave a tremendous shock to the abolitionists. Let us be thankful to God, and give him the glory. And now, if we of the south and they of the north, whose sympathies are with us, shall be mild, I am satisfied that abolitionism will go down among Baptists. All our "principal men" are sound to the core on this <i>vexed question</i>.
<p>"The Triennial Convention exhibited a noble spectacle of moral grandeur. About two hundred and fifty men from the various parts of our extended country were engaged in a long and arduous session, that tried the temper and put into requisition all the intellectual energy which they possessed. And all this in connection with a most exciting subject.  And yet, self-possession, calmness, the Christian spirit, predominated throughout the whole scene. No tumult, no angry feeling, no harsh expression had place in our deliberations and conclusions. At the communion board on Lord's day the scene was overwhelming. In view of the cross the hundreds that participated were all one. No test, other than that of our dear Lord's requirement, was thought of. To God be all the glory, Amen and Amen.</p></blockquote>
<p>But such dissatisfaction arose among the <i>now</i> "principal men" in the convention that before the next Triennial gathering a division occurred.   A new but small rival society was formed. One principal reason assigned being that gifts of slave-holders should not mingle with northern contributions in the missionary treasury, since God had said "<a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?Isaiah+61:8">I hate robbery for a burnt offering</a>."
</p><p></p><center>-408-</center><hr>

<p>But the extent of principle and height of integrity of this new and sublimated movement, was seen in the fact, that when, just afterwards, the old board sustained a loss by a failure in India, there was an immediate appropriation of five hundred dollars voted to it, with all its slavery, out of this purified treasury. The following is the official record of the proceeding:
</p><blockquote>"<i>Whereas</i>, The Foreign Mission Board have recently sustained a heavy loss, by the failure of their banker at Calcutta, and thus appropriated supplies are cut off from the missionaries in Asia; therefore
<p>"<i>Resolved</i>, That the treasurer of this committee be instructed to forward, as soon as possible, five hundred dollars from funds now in the treasury, to the relief of the missionaries, "to be expended under the direction of Dr. Judson and Mr. Vinton."
</p><p></p><center><dd>[Signed]   S. G. SHIPLEY, <i>Chairman</i>.
</dd><dd>C.W. DENISON, <i>Secretary</i>."</dd></center></blockquote>
<p>The new association seems to have been short lived, for at the next meeting of the old board, all parties old and new were present, and the proceedings were as unanimous almost as before slavery had ever disturbed them. The president, a North Carolina slave-holder, declined a reelection, on the ground that, as for more than thirty years the chief officer had been selected from the slave states, it was time the boon should be conferred on the north. Accordingly, the Rev. Dr. [Francis] Wayland, of Providence, on the second ballotting, was elected to that office.
</p><p>The subject of Slavery was introduced and disposed of by the passage of the following resolution, <font size="2">ONLY TWO</font><b> voting in the negative:
</b></p><blockquote><b>"<i>Resolved</i>, That cooperating together as members of this convention in the work of foreign missions, we disclaim all sanction, either express or implied, of <i>slavery or anti-slavery</i>; but as individuals we are free to express and promote our view on this or other subjects, in a Christian manner and spirit."</b></blockquote><b>
<p></p><center>-409-</center><hr>

<p>Rev. Mr. Davis, of New York, [neighbor of Elon Galusha] then remarked with much exultation, if not <i>exaltation</i>, that the convention had passed <i>a stupendous crisis</i> and moved a season of devotional exercises. The season was voted, a northern minister, Mr. Webb, of Philadelphia, gave thanks, and they closed with singing the Doxology, by the congregation,
</p><p></p><center>"Praise God, from whom all blessings flow."</center>
<p>In view of the passage of the above resolution, the editor of the <i>Boston Christian Reflector</i>, a professedly anti-slavery journal, most complacently remarked:
</p><blockquote>"It will be seen by the passage of the resolution on Friday, that we are no longer required to fellowship slavery or slave-holders, as such, in the work of missions."</blockquote>
<p>But had the business related to <i>infant sprinkling</i> instead of infant stealing, or on <i>immersion</i> as baptism, instead of <i>sprinkling</i>, the whole past history of this immense denomination throughout Christendom proves it would never have been so easily nor so amicably adjusted.
</p><p>In 1846, a new association was incorporated under the name of "The American Baptist Missionary Union," and the old <i>triennial</i> was no more.  The first article of the new constitution designates the name, the second the object of the society. The third provides that "<i>persons," without reference to place</i>, "may be life members, by the payment at one time of not less than one hundred dollars." The twenty-first article declares that the officers and missionaries of the association "shall be members in good standing of regular Baptist churches." No north nor south any more.
</p><p>It has been contended that this association was formed with particular reference to a separation from slavery. I was so informed <i>by an officer of the board</i>.
</p><p></p><center>-410-</center><hr>

<p>But there was no such intimation, either in the act of incorporation or the constitution.  Among the life members were persons from Missouri, Mississippi, Delaware, and Georgia; and the first meeting of the board of managers was organized by the choice of a president from a free, and a secretary from a slave state. The first meeting of the union was opened with prayer by the Rev. Nathaniel Colver, of Boston. All these proceedings, and others, are appended to the annual report of the old Baptist convention for 1846.
</p><p>But enough about the mission movements as between south and north, or between slavery and anti-slavery. The Baptist denomination, like the others, had hosts of anti-slavery men and women; but the <i>ruling power</i> was for slavery, or the system could not have survived as it did, till stove down by the avenging bolts of eternal wrath.
</p><p><a name="p411mec"></a></p><center><font size="4">METHODIST EPISCOPAL CHURCH.</font></center><b>
<p>Coming now to the mighty Methodist denomination it would be a relief and joy if a far better record could be given. But to write history, not make it, is the work still in hand.
</p><p>Two copies of the Methodist Book of Discipline are before me, of different dates, but both contain an address "To the Members of the Methodist Episcopal Church," signed by the bishops, in which it is declared:
</p><blockquote>"We wish to see this little publication in the house of every Methodist. And the more so, as it contains the articles of religion, maintained more or less, in part, or in whole, by every reformed church in the world. Far from wishing you to be ignorant of any of our doctrines, or any part of our discipline, we wish you to read, mark, learn, and inwardly digest the whole. You ought, next to the word of God, to procure the</blockquote>
<p></p><center>-411-</center><hr>


<p></p><center><img src="undercon.gif" width="128" height="149"><br>(pp 412-425)</center><p>

</p><center><a name="p426"></a><font size="4">THE PROTESTANT EPISCOPAL CHURCH.</font></center>
<p>The anti-slavery character of this denomination is pretty clearly set forth in a pamphlet, entitled, "<i>Thoughts on the duty of the Episcopal church in relation to slavery</i>," by the late <s>William</s> [John] Jay, one of its most illustrious members:
</p><p></p><center><table width="498" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>Alas! for the expectation that she would conform to the spirit of her ancient mother. <p>She has not only remained a mute and careless spectator of this great conflict of truth and justice with hypocrisy and cruelty, but her very priests and deacons may be seen ministering at the altar of slavery! Offering their talents and influence at its unholy shrine, and openly repeating the awful blasphemy that the precepts of our Savior sanction the system of American slavery!
</p><p>Her northern, free-state clergy, with rare exceptions, whatever they may feel on the subiect, rebuke it neither in public nor in private.
</p><p>And her periodicals, far from advancing the progress of abolitionism, at times oppose our societies; impliedly defending slavery, as not incompatible with christianity, and, occasionally withholding information useful to the cause of freedom.</p></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>

<p>As why should they not, or, rather, it might be asked, how could they have done otherwise? pulpit, or press, with instructions like the following, <a name="p426-obletter"></a>issued by the oldest bishop in the United States, for their instruction and guidance, though directed, as will be seen, to a far more august dignitary than the bishop himself:
</p><p></p><center><table width="320" border="3" bgcolor="ddffff"><tbody><tr><td><b>Ed. Note: Pillsbury had reprinted this more fully in 1847, in <i>Forlorn Church</i>, <a href="forlorn.htm#p51-obletter">pp 51-53</a>; and see related parody, <a href="#p326-parody">pp 326-327</a>, <i>supra</i>.</b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>

<p></p><center><table width="498" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>
<dd></dd><dd></dd><dd></dd><dd></dd><dd>JUBILEE COLLEGE, ILLINOIS, N. A., }
</dd><dd></dd><dd></dd><dd></dd><dd></dd><dd></dd><dd></dd><dd></dd><dd>August 1, A. D. 1846. &nbsp; }
</dd>

<p>To the Right Rev. Samuel, Lord Bishop of Oxford, England;
</p><p>VERY DEAR BROTHER IN THE LORD JESUS—Allow me, the oldest bishop of the "Protestant Episcopal church" in the United States, to address your lordship on the subject of a pamphlet, entitled "<i>A Re-</i></p></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p></p><center>-426-</center><hr>

<center><table width="500" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b><i>proof of the American Church</i>," which "reproof" is said to be contained in copious "extracts" from your lordship's lately published history of said church.
<p></p><center><table width="320" border="3" bgcolor="ddffff"><tbody><tr><td><b>Ed. Note: Full citation: Samuel Wilberforce, M.A. (1805-1873), <i>A Reproof of the American Church by the Bishop of Oxford, extracted from a "History of the Protestant Episcopal Church in America</i>" (New York, W. Harned, 1846), retitled and reprinted as, <i>A Reproof of the American Church on the Subject of Slavery</i> (London: W. Tweedie, 1853)
<hr>See also Samuel Wilberforce, and Evan Malbone Johnson, <i>A History of the Protestant Episcopal Church in America</i> (New York, Stanford and Swords, 1849)
<hr>Reprints show this letter did not persuade.</b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>Never having read this work from which the said "reproof" is drawn, yet from many years' acquaintance with your lordship's excellent character, I can say, with full confidence, that the <i>acerbity</i> which is spread over the pages of this pamphlet cannot be approved by your lordship.<font size="3"> * * *</font>
</p><p>In the deepest sorrow of heart do I lament the melancholy effects produced by the circumstances before me. Alas! what do I see? The bishops and clergy of America censured for that of which they are not guilty, and of which they are not the cause, and those who censure them evidently unconscious both of the evils which their mistaken censure produces, and of the extent of the evils which must follow from the weight of their character and opinion.
</p><p></p><center><table width="250" border="3" bgcolor="ddffff"><tbody><tr><td><b>Ed. Note: Yes, people will realize American churches are <a href="thieves.htm#the-charges">villainous</a>.</b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>Before I proceed, I beg leave to state, that, in endeavoring, by mv feeble means, to shield the Protestant Episcopal church in the United States, I crave to be understood as not assuming political ground.
</p><p>The Episcopal church in America did in no wise originate slavery. She always, in connection with other benevolent persons of the day, raised her voice against its introduction into the then British colonies.
</p><p>Nor is she now, in any competent sense, a part of the civil government to cure its temporal evils. Her bishops are not, as the English prelates are, admitted to a seat in the halls of legislation, nor are they allowed to "rise in their places" to plead the cause of humanity.
</p><p>All she can do [says this fatalist] is by her prayers and the preaching of the gospel, and teaching the blessed doctrines of christianity, to endeavor to ameliorate the condition of the slave; but, like the primitive christians amidst the evils that surround her, she does not think herself called upon to eradicate at once the evil.
</p><p></p><center><table width="310" border="3" bgcolor="ddffff"><tbody><tr><td><b>Ed. Note: See rebuttal by Harriet B. Stowe, <i>Key</i> (1853), <a href="keytom.htm#p205-ii">pp 205-223</a> and <a href="keytom.htm#p228-iv">228-243</a>.
<hr>Note that this immoral man refuses to say, the Church can
<li>teach that slavery is a sin
</li><li>stop retaining and promoting slavers
</li><li>follow <a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?1Corinthians+5:1-5">1 Cor. 5:1, 4-5</a> precedent, excom-municating blatant sinners, slaveholders.
<hr>Remember pro-slavery clergy motive, <a href="thieves.htm#p71-vile-clergy">concubines for themselves</a>.</li></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>She [the Church] rather finds herself commanded, as were the servants in the gospel, to exercise caution, "lest in eradicating the tares, they root out the wheat also."  Let both grow together, saith our Lord. [<a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?Matthew+13:24-30">Matthew 13:29-30</a>]. Let the <i>evil</i> be borne [retained] for the sake of the <i>good</i> that may be done to the souls of the poor slaves.
</p><p></p><center><table width="286" border="2" bgcolor="ddffff"><tbody><tr><td><b>Ed. Note: Not so. The "wheat and tares" message does not mean
<li>admitting into the church persons with an unrepentant lifestyle, overt, blatant sinful acts, or harming-others behavior
</li><li>don't warn people away from sin.
<hr>The end of "tares" is burning, referring to the blatant unrepentant.</li></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center><br></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p></p><center>-427-</center><hr>

<center><table width="520" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>"The tenor of these remarks falls in with the example of St. Paul.
<p></p><center><table width="300" border="2" bgcolor="ddffff"><tbody><tr><td><b>Ed. Note: Not so.  Paul set an example of excommunicating the unrepentant sinner. <a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?1Corinthians+5:1-5">1 Cor. 1:1, 4-5</a>, as per <a href="http://bible.gospelcom.net/bible?Matthew+18:15-17">Matthew 18:15-17</a>.</b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>"The gospel tbrough his mouth, and the power of the divine Spirit, had converted the noble Philemon from the slavery of sin to the freedom of the Son of God.
</p><p>"This Philemon's '<i>run-away slave</i>' enjoyed the same benefit at the hands of the same apostle, some time after, while a prisoner in the city of Rome. His name was <i>Onesimus</i>, and while ministering to the necessities of the holy apostle, he heard the word of God, and like his master, believed.
</p><p>"It now becomes a matter of great importance, in relation to the subject of this letter, to know what directions the apostle gave to the converted slave of Philemon, when he sent him back to his master.
</p><p>"Was it that he was a freeman in the temporal sense, and must maintain his rights as a part of 'a whole gospel'? Was it that as a freeman he was to go back and claim the privileges and immunities of this his temporal freedom, as it is now understood by the abolitionists?
</p><p>"Was it that henceforth he was to consider himself as having a right to propagate his sentiments and "preach the whole gospel'?
</p><p>"That is to say, that he had a right to creep into his former master's kitchen and fill the heads of all the [alleged] bond-servants with the ideas of their temporal rights according to this creed, thereby exciting them to rebellion, and if resisted (and resisted they certainly would be), to murder their kind master and take possession of his estate?
</p><p>"Far, very far from so wicked an estimate of the holy religion unto the blessings and privileges of which the apostle had admitted him, this now converted servant of the pious Philemon, that he sent the former immediately back to serve the latter as heretofore.
</p><p>"Not a word of <i>abolitionism</i> was uttered in the presence of <i>Onesimus</i>, or intimated by the apostle.
</p><p></p><center><table width="470" border="1" bgcolor="ddffff"><tbody><tr><td><b>Ed. Note: For honest analysis of <i>Philemon</i>, see
<li>Rev. John Fee, <i>Anti-Slavery Manual</i> (1851), <a href="feeasm1851.htm#philemon">pp 109-110</a>
</li><li>Rev. George B. Cheever, <i>God Against Slavery</i> (1857), <a href="cheevergvs.htm#p140">pp 140-147</a>
</li><li>Rev. Stephen Foster, <i>Thieves</i> (1843), <a href="thieves.htm#philemon">pp 48-49</a>.</li></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>"He [Paul] entreats Philemon to receive his servant back again as a brother beloved of Christ, though still a servant, and as such, if required, engages to pay the losses he had occasioned his master by his leaving him.
</p><p>"'If he hath wronged thee aught, put that to my account, I Paul have written it with my own hand, I will repay it.' 
</p><p>"How different this from the language of modern abolitionism! Yet this, my Lord, is a part of our Holy Bible."</p></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p></p><center>-428-</center><hr>

<center><table width="498" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>"Hence it is clearly to be inferred [assumed] that the [<a href="cheevergvs.htm">sinful</a>] relations of political society are to continue, be they what they may, notwithstanding the most intimate ties of Christian fellowship."
<p></p><center><table width="310" border="1" bgcolor="ddffff"><tbody><tr><td><b>Ed. Note: The letter-writer disregards

<p>Here is another singularly illustrative act, furnished, too, as is all the testimony introduced—by the [<a href="pillsburypacts.htm#p377-ed-note">pretended Christian</a>] church itself.
</p><p>In 1836 Rev. George W. Freeman delivered two sermons in Raleigh, North Carolina, that were published under the imposing title of "<i>The Rights and Duties of Slave-holders</i>," with the following imprimatur from Bishop Ives, of the diocese:
</p><p></p><center><table width="498" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>
<center><dd></dd><dd></dd><dd>"RALEIGH, Nov. 30, 1836.</dd></center>
<p>"REV. AND DEAR BROTHER:—I listened with most unfeigned pleasure to the discourses delivered last Sunday, on the <a href="goodellsaas.htm#p27-john-wesley">character of slavery</a> and the duties of <a href="abolitionists.htm#are-no-masters">masters</a>.
</p><p>"And as I learn a publication of them is solicited, I beg, from a conviction of their being urgently called for at the present time, that you will not withhold your consent.
</p><p>"Your affectionate friend and brother in the Lord,
</p><p></p><center><dd></dd><dd></dd><dd>"L. S. IVES."</dd></center></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>In South Carolina, the "Society for the Advancement of Christianity," made up of clergymen and laymen, the bishop at the head of it, seized upon the sermons, imprimatur and all, and published them as religious tracts, for gratuitous distribution.
</p><p></p><center><table width="302" border="1" bgcolor="ddffff"><tbody><tr><td><b>Ed. Note: Full citation: George W. Freeman (1789-1858), <i>The Rights and Duties of Slaveholders: Two Discourses Delivered on Sunday, November 27, 1836, in Christ Church, Raleigh, North Carolina</i> (Raleigh: J. Gales &amp; son, 1836)</b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>An extract from the [<a href="patton1846.htm#listvileclergy">vile</a> and <a href="pillsburypacts.htm#p377-ed-note">heathen</a>, pro-slavery] sermons read thus:
</p><p></p><center><table width="498" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>"No man or set of men in our day, unless they can produce a new Revelation from heaven, are entitled to pronounce slavery wrong.  * * *
<p></p><center><table border="1" bgcolor="ddffff"><tbody><tr><td><b>Ed. Note: Rejecting <a href="keytom.htm#p115">the current revelation</a>!</b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>

<p><a name="p429-not-gospel"></a>And now one more witness, perhaps most valuable of all, and a late bishop, too. On my table is a work with this imposing title:
</p><center><table width="440"><tbody><tr><td><b><i>Sermons addressed to masters and and servants and published in the year 1743, by Rev. Thomas Bacon</i> [c. 1700-1768], <i>minister</i></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>

<center><table width="400"><tbody><tr><td><b><i>of the Protestant Episcopal Church in Maryland, now republished with other tracts and dialogues on the same subject, and recommended to all masters and mistresses to be used in their families. <p></p><center><table width="310" border="3" bgcolor="ddffff"><tbody><tr><td><b>Ed. Note: Pillsbury had reprinted an excerpt in 1847, in <i>Forlorn Church</i>, <a href="forlorn.htm#p14-not-gospel">pp 14-18</a>; and see the parody, <a href="#p326-parody">pp 326-327</a>, <i>supra</i>.</b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>In his preface, Bishop [William] Meade [1789-1862] remarks:
</p><p></p><center><table width="498" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>"The editor of this volume offers it to all masters and mistresses in our southern states, with the anxious wish and devout prayer that it may prove a blessing to themselves and their households.
<p>"He considers himself most happy in having met with the several pieces which compose it, and could not with a quiet conscience refrain from affording to others the opportunity of profiting thereby."</p></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>The title of this work shows its miscellaneous character. The sermons are in two series, the first, doubtless, by Mr. Bacon published in 1743. Then succeed two others, author not named, but presumably by Bishop Meade himself, and always so assigned while he lived [1789-1862]. And from them the following excerpts are taken.
</p><p>He first [purportedly] shows that God appointed, for great and wonderfui ends [purposes], several offices and degrees in his family, making some masters and mistresses, some kings and rulers, some merchants and sea-faring men, some tradesmen, husbandmen and planters and laboring men to work for their own living, and some He hath made <i>servants and slaves</i> to assist and work for their masters and mistresses who provide for them.
</p><p></p><center><table width="320" border="3" bgcolor="ddffff"><tbody><tr><td><b>Ed. Note: The <a href="patton1846.htm#listvileclergy">vile</a>,

<hr>America <a href="slaveryillegal.htm#anti-monarchypolicy">rejected monarchy/aristocracy</a>.</li></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p>And as God hath sent each of us into the world for some or other of these purposes, we are all obliged, from the king to the poorest slave, to do the business He hath set us about.
</p><p>And while you whom He hath made slaves are honestly and quietly doing your business and living as poor christians ought to live, you are serving God in your low station as much as the greatest prince alive.
</p><p></p><center>-430-</center><hr>

<p>With more in the same strain, but not one duty specified, not one grace, not one emotion nor aspiration that rises above, or relates to any power or person, only the master and mistress and their service and adoration, as what follows, in the recent bishop's own words, abundantly shows:
</p><p></p><center><table width="498" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>"When people die, we know of but two places they have to go to, and one is heaven, the other hell. Now heaven is a place of great happiness, which God has prepared for all that are good, where they shall enjoy rest from their labors.
<p>"And hell is a place of great torment and misery, where all wicked people will be shut up with the devil and other evil spirits, and be punished forever, because they will not serve God.
</p><p>"If, therefore we would have our souls saved hy Christ, if we would escape hell and obtain heaven, we must set about doing what he requires of us, that is, to serve God.
</p><p>"Your own poor circumstances in this life ought to put <i>you</i> particularly upon this, and taking care of your souls. * *
</p><p>"Almighty God hath been <a href="abolitionists.htm#are-no-masters">pleased to make you slaves</a> here, and to give you nothing but labor and poverty in this world, which you are obliged to submit to, as it is his will that it should be so.
</p><p>"And think within yourselves what a terrible thing it would be, after all your labors and sufferings in this life, to be turned into hell in the next life: and after wearing out your bodies in service here, to go into a far worse slavery when this is over, and your poor souls be delivered over into the possession of the devil, to become his slaves forever in hell, without any hope of ever getting free from it.
</p><p>"If, therefore, you would be God's freemen in heaven, you must strive to be good and serve him here on earth.
</p><p>"Your bodies, you know, are not your own: they are at the disposal of those you belong to; but your precious souls are still your own, which nothing can take from you, if it  be not your own fault.
</p><p>"Consider well, then, that if you lose your souls by leading idle, wicked lives here, you have got nothing by it in this world, and you have lost your all in the next. For your idleness and wickedness are generally found out, and your</p></b></td></tr></tbody></table></center>
<p></p><center>-431-</center><hr>

<center><table width="498" bgcolor="ddeeee"><tbody><tr><td><b>bodies suffer for it here; and, what is far worse, if you do not repent and amend, your unhappy souls will suffer for it hereafter.
<p>"Having thus shown you the chief duties you owe to your great Master in heaven, I now come to lay before you the duties you owe to your masters and mistresses here upon earth.
</p><p></p><center><table width="390" border="1" bgcolor="ddffff"><tbody><tr><td><b>Ed. Note: This <a href="patton1846.htm#listvileclergy">vile</a> individual carefully avoids offering <i>proof</i>,

<p>"And for this you have one general rule that you ought always to carry in your minds, and that is, to <i>do all service for them, as if you did it for God himself</i>.
</p><p>"Poor creatures! you little consider when you are idle and neglectful of your master's business, when you steal and waste, and hurt any of their substance, when you are saucy and impudent, when you are telling them lies and deceiving them, or when you prove stubborn and sullen, and will not do the work you are set about without stripes and vexation; you do not consider, I say, that what faults you are guilty of towards your masters and mistresses, are faults done against God himself who hath set your masters and mistresses over you in his own stead, and expects that you will do for them just as you would do for him.
